Ruling on Salam in Animals
Refutes analogy and cites hadith to prohibit salam in animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in animals and explain both the rational (qiyas ma'a al-fariq) and textual (hadith) proofs.

Core Idea
Salam in animals is prohibited due to excessive hidden disparity and the prophetic prohibition.

This passage addresses a crucial question in Islamic sales: can you pay in advance for an animal to be delivered later? Some argued it should be allowed like cloth. The Hanafi school refutes this by exposing a fatal flaw in the analogy and citing a direct hadith from the Prophet. You will learn the reasoning and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the ruling being debated? The permissibility of salam in animals.
2
Understand the opponent's qiyas
They analogize animals to cloth: if described, disparity is slight, so salam should be allowed.
This is the reasoning that the Hanafi text sets up to refute.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation (qiyas ma'a al-fariq)
Animals have hidden qualities (beauty, temperament) causing gross disparity, unlike man-made cloth which is uniform.
The key distinction: Allah's creation vs. human manufacture.
4
Examine the textual evidence
The hadith from Ibn Abbas: the Prophet forbade salaf (salam) in animals.
The hadith is considered sahih by al-Hakim.
5
Derive the final hukm
Salam in animals is haram (prohibited) in the Hanafi school.
Both rational and textual proofs converge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الحيوان ؛ لأنه يتعين بذكرِ الجنس والنوع والصفة. قلنا : في ذلـك فـحـش التفاوت، (وأطرافه ) : كالرُّؤوس والأكارع" كالسمين والهزال، والتفاوت بعد هذا البيان يسير، فأشبه الحيوان بالنّياب، فإنَّ فيها بعد ذكر الذرع والصفة والنوع لا يبقى إلا تفاوت يسير، فيجوز السلم فيها، فينبغي أن يجوز هاهنا أيضا. [١] قوله : قلنا؛ حاصلُهُ أنَّ قياس الحيوانِ على الثياب قياس مع الفارق ؛ لأنَّ في الحيوان بعد بيان الجنس والنوع والصفة تفاوتاً فاحشاً، باعتبار المعاني الباطنة كالصباحةِ والملاحة وغيرهما مما لا دخل فيه لأحد، فهذا التفاوت يفضي إلى المنازعة بخلاف الثياب، فإنّها من صنيع العباد. فإذا اتَّحد الصانع والآلةَ اتَّحد الصنيع، ولا يتفاوت في المالية إلا قليلاً، والحيوانُ صنيع الله ؛ وذلك يكون على ما يريدُهُ تعالى، فقد كان على وجه لا يوجد له نظير، وفي مثلِهِ لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ بالاتفاق، هذا مُلَخَّصُ ما قرَّرهُ العلامةُ العَيْنِي ولنا أيضاً : ما أخرجه الحاكم في «المستدرك»، والدارقطني في «سننه»: عن ابن عباس أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : نهى عن السَّلَفِ في الحيوان (۲)، قال الحاكم : حديث صحيح الإسناد، ولم يخرجاه، ذكره العلامة الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في تخريجه» (۳).
Regarding animals: because it is determined by mentioning genus, type, and description. We said: in that there is gross disparity. (And its extremities): like heads and trotters, and fat and lean, and the disparity after this description is slight, so it resembles animals in comparison to [؟]? For verily in them after mentioning length, description, and type, only slight disparity remains, so salam is permissible in them. So it should be permissible here as well. [1] His statement: We said; its summary is that analogizing animals to cloth is an analogy with a difference; because in animals after describing genus, type, and description there is gross disparity, considering hidden qualities like beauty and comeliness and others that no one can control, so this disparity leads to dispute, unlike cloth, for it is the work of servants. When the maker and tool are the same, the product is the same, and there is only slight disparity in value. Animals are the work of Allah; that is as He wills, so it may be in a manner that has no equal, and in such cases salam is not permissible by consensus. This is the summary of what Allamah al-Ayni explained. We also have: what al-Hakim narrated in al-Mustadrak and al-Daraqutni in his Sunan from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet ﷺ forbade salaf in animals. Al-Hakim said: the hadith is sahih al-isnad, though al-Bukhari and Muslim did not narrate it. Allamah al-Zaylai mentioned it in his takhrij.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on salam in animals. It begins by addressing an opponent's argument: that if the animal is described by genus, type, and description, the remaining disparity is slight, similar to cloth or houses, so salam should be allowed. The Hanafi response (Qulna) refutes the analogy: animals have hidden qualities (beauty, temperament) that cause gross disparity, unlike man-made items which are uniform. Additionally, a hadith from Ibn Abbas is cited: the Prophet forbade salaf (salam) in animals. This establishes the ruling as prohibited. The passage also notes that Imam al-Ayni summarized the rational argument, and al-Zaylai verified the hadith's authenticity. The ruling is clear: salam in animals is not valid due to both rational (gross disparity) and textual (hadith) evidence.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A sale with advance payment for future delivery of specified goods.
التفاوت (al-tafawut): Disparity in value or quality among individuals of the same type.
القياس مع الفارق (al-qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that fails because the original and new cases differ in a material way.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary textual evidence cited for the prohibition of salam in animals?
A) A verse from the Quran
B) A hadith from Ibn Abbas forbidding salaf in animals
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Analogy to cloth

Key Takeaway
Salam in animals is prohibited in the Hanafi school due to excessive hidden disparity and the prophetic prohibition.

Salam in Animal Limbs: The Disparity Condition
Explains ruling on salam in limbs with alternative view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in animal limbs and identify the condition under which an alternative view permits it.

Core Idea
Salam in animal limbs is invalid due to significant disparity, but may be permitted if sold by weight with specification.

Salam is a forward sale where the price is paid now and the goods delivered later. A key condition for validity is that the subject matter be precisely defined without significant variation. This lesson examines why salam in animal limbs like heads and hooves is generally not allowed, and explores a minority Hanafi view that permits it when sold by weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, recognize that the act is salam (forward sale) in animal limbs such as heads (ru'us) and hooves/trotters (akari'). State the ruling: it is not permissible (makruh tahrimi) according to the majority Hanafi position.
2
Understand the rationale (illah)
The reason for impermissibility is al-tafawut al-mu'tabar (significant disparity). These limbs vary greatly in size and meat content, leading to uncertainty and potential dispute. Note that uncertainty (gharar) in the subject matter invalidates salam.
The disparity is considered significant among people (tafawutuha mu'tabar bayna al-nas).
3
Locate the alternative view
The text cites a view from al-Fath that permits salam in heads and trotters provided: (1) they are sold by weight (waznan), (2) the type is specified, and (3) all other conditions of salam are met. The basis is that when weighed and specified, the disparity is no longer gross (fahish).
4
Analyse the condition for the alternative
The key condition is 'by weight' (waznan). Weight standardizes the amount, reducing disparity. Without weight, the limbs are too varied. Also, specifying the type (e.g., sheep head vs. goat head) removes ambiguity.
If sold by count, disparity remains, so the alternative does not apply.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is impermissibility. However, the permissive view from al-Fath may be followed in need, especially when weight and specification are present. Recognize that this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : وأطرافه ; وإنما لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ فيها لكونها متفاوتة، وتفاوتها معتبر بين النَّاس، فتفضي إلى النزاع ، وقال في «الفتح» ) : وعندي لا بأس بالسلم في الرؤوس والأكارع وزنا بعد ذكر النوع وباقي الشروط، فإنَّها من جنس واحد، وحينئذ لا تتفاوت تفاوتاً فاحشاً. انتهى. [٣] قوله : والأكارع ; قال في «الصراح»: كراعُ بالضم: بارجه گوسفند و گاوو جزآن، وهو بمنزلة الوظيف في الفرس والبعير، وهو مشدق الساق، يذكر ويؤنث : أكرع وأكارع ج. انتهى.
[2] His statement: 'and its extremities'; indeed salam in them is not allowed because they are disparate, and their disparity is considered significant among people, so it leads to dispute. And it is said in al-Fath: 'In my view there is no objection to salam in heads and trotters by weight after mentioning the type and remaining conditions, for they are of one kind, and then they do not differ grossly.' End. [3] His statement: 'and al-akari'; it is said in al-Sirah: 'Kura' (with dammah): sheep or cow leg, like the shin of a horse or camel, the flesh of the leg; masculine and feminine; plural akru' and akari.'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation on salam in animal limbs (ru'us and akari' - heads and hooves/trotters). The base ruling is that salam in such parts is not valid because they vary significantly among people (size, meat content), leading to disputes. However, a quote from al-Fath (possibly al-Kasani or another) presents an alternative: if they are sold by weight after specifying type and conditions, they are of one kind and the disparity is not gross, so salam is allowed. The passage then provides a lexical definition of akari' as animal legs, clarifying the term. The operative ruling in the madhhab is the majority view: prohibition, but the exception by weight may be followed.

Key Terms
الأكارع (al-akari'): The hooves or trotters of animals, specifically the shank of sheep or cow.
التفاوت المعتبر (al-tafawut al-mu'tabar): Significant disparity that is considered legally relevant, leading to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When sold by count
B) When sold by weight after specifying type and conditions
C) When sold fresh
D) When sold with the whole animal

Key Takeaway
Salam in animal limbs is impermissible due to significant disparity, but may be valid if sold by weight and type specified, per a minority Hanafi view.

The Hanafi Ruling on Salam in Animal Skins
Inter-madhhab dispute on salam in animal skins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on salam in animal skins, explain the rationale of disparity and dispute, and contrast it with the Maliki view.

Core Idea
Salam in skins is prohibited due to size disparity in count, but allowed if type is specified.

This lesson examines the validity of salam (advance payment sale) in animal skins. The Hanafi school generally prohibits it because skins are sold by number with significant size variation, leading to disputes. However, Imam Malik permits it if weighed and described. We will explore the reasoning and a concession within the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? Salam in animal skins. The Hanafi ruling is that it is not permissible (makruh tahrimi).
2
Understand the Hanafi argument
Skins are customarily sold by number, not weight. Small and large skins vary greatly, causing uncertainty that leads to dispute.
Dispute (niza') invalidates the contract.
3
Examine the Maliki objection
Imam Malik argues that skins can be weighed and described, thus they are known and deliverable, making salam valid.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal is that custom overrides theoretical possibility: because skins are not usually weighed, the weight-based argument fails. The primary custom is sale by number, which creates disparity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, salam in skins is generally invalid unless the type is clearly specified (exception from al-Dhakhirah). Under Maliki law, it is valid if weighed and described.
This difference affects real transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجلوده عدداً، والحطب حُزَماً ، والرَّطْبَةِ جُرَزْاً، والجواهر، والخَرَز، وبصاع وذراع معين لم يدر قدره
[١]قوله : وجلوده ; أي لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ في جلودِ الحيوان، وقال في «النهاية»: قال مالك له : يجوز؛ لأنه مقدورُ التسليم، معلوم المقدار بالوزن والصفة، ولكنا نقول : الجلود لا توزن عادةً ، فلم يجز وزناً بالطريق الأولى، ولكنها تباع عدداً، متفاوت فيها الصغير والكبير، فلا يجوزُ السَّلّم فيها ؛ لأنه مفض إلى المنازعة. انتهى. وفي «الذخيرة»: إنَّ بيَّن للجلود ضرباً معلوماً يجوز؛ لانتفاء المنازعة، كذا في «العناية» (٢).
And its skins by number, and firewood by bundles, and fresh grass by bundles, and jewels, and beads, and by a sa' and a specific cubit whose measure is unknown. [1] His statement: 'and its skins'; meaning salam in the skins of animals is not allowed. And it is said in al-Nihayah: 'Malik said: it is allowed because it is possible to deliver, known in measure by weight and description. But we say: skins are not usually weighed, so they are not permissible by weight in the primary way. Rather, they are sold by number, with the small and large varying, so salam in them is not allowed because it leads to dispute.' End. And in al-Dhakhirah: 'If the type of skin is specified and known, it is allowed due to the absence of dispute.' Likewise in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an inter-madhhab dispute regarding salam in animal skins. The Hanafi position is that salam in skins is not allowed because they are customarily sold by count (number), and there is significant disparity between small and large skins, leading to potential disputes. Imam Malik is reported to have allowed it, arguing that skins can be weighed and described to eliminate uncertainty. The Hanafi response counters that skins are not normally weighed, so the weight-based argument fails. However, a concession is noted from al-Dhakhirah: if the specific type of skin is clearly specified, then salam may be allowed because the source of dispute is removed. This shows flexibility within the madhhab.

Key Terms
الجلود (al-julud): Animal skins, tanned or raw; the subject of the salam contract in this ruling.
النزاع (al-niza'): Dispute between buyer and seller; a key factor that invalidates salam in Hanafi law.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid immediately for future delivery of defined goods.
الذخيرة (al-Dhakhirah): A Hanafi fiqh reference work by al-Bukhari (not the Hadith scholar) – cited here for the exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because skins are always sold by number, not weight
B) Because weighing is not accurate
C) Because the Prophet forbade it
D) Because skins are not considered merchandise

Key Takeaway
Salam in animal skins is generally prohibited in the Hanafi school due to disparity in size when sold by number, but if the specific type is clearly specified, it becomes permissible.

Salam in Firewood and Fresh Grass: Default Prohibition and Condition for Permissibility
Explains prohibition of salam in firewood and grass sold by bundles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam in firewood and fresh grass and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Salam in bundles is void due to uncertainty, unless the bundle length is specified to remove disparity.

Salam is a sale where the price is paid upfront for goods to be delivered later. For such a contract to be valid, the commodity must be precisely defined. This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a specific case: firewood and fresh grass sold in bundles. The default ruling is prohibition due to size variation, but an exception is made when the bundle tie length is specified, removing the uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the goods
Recognize that salam is a forward sale. The goods here are firewood (hatab) and fresh grass (ratbah), sold in bundles (huzam/juzar).
Bundles are natural units that vary in size, causing uncertainty.
2
Determine the default ruling
The text states it is not allowed (la yajuz) due to disparity (al-tafāwut). Understand that disparity (jahālah) in quantity or quality invalidates salam.
The default ruling is prohibition (fasid/batil).
3
Identify the exception and its condition
The exception is conditional: if the length of the bundle tie (mā yushaddu bihi al-huzmah) is specified, then it becomes permitted. This specification removes the uncertainty.
The condition is specifying a measurable dimension (length), not just weight or volume.
4
Apply the principle to similar goods
Any salam where the commodity is sold in similar variable units (e.g., piles, heaps) follows the same rule: default prohibited unless a controlling specification removes disparity.
This is a specific example of the general Hanafi requirement of precise description in salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحطب حُزَماً ، والرَّطْبَةِ جُرَزْاً.
الحزم : جمع الحزمة، وهي بالفارسية «بندهيزم». والجرز : جمع الجرزة، وهي بالفارسية «دسته تره».
وإنما لا يجوز في الحطب للتَّفاوت حتى إن بَيَّنَ طول ما يُشَدُّ به الحزمة يجوز (۳)

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on salam in firewood (hatab) and fresh grass (ratbah) when sold in bundles (huzam, jurz). The default is that salam is not valid because bundles can vary in size and content, leading to disparity. However, the text adds a conditional exception: if the length of the bundle (the tie) is specified, then salam becomes permissible because the disparity is controlled. The passage also provides Persian glosses for the terms huzmah and jurzah, indicating the scholastic context.

الجرزة (al-jurzah): A bundle of fresh grass; Persian: 'dasteh tara'.
التفاوت (al-tafāwut): Disparity, variation in size or quantity causing uncertainty.

Salam in Jewels and Beads: The Principle of Uniformity
Ruling with reason (talil) distinguishing valid and invalid salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between salam contracts that are invalid due to gross disparity in value and those that are valid because the item is standardized by weight.

Core Idea
Salam requires known value; gross disparity in individual items invalidates the contract.

In salam (advance payment sale), the commodity must be precisely specified to avoid uncertainty. Jewels and large beads are unique and vary greatly in value, making salam invalid. However, small pearls sold by weight are uniform, so salam is permissible. This lesson explores the underlying rationale and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the ruling for salam in jewels/beads and for small pearls. The text says it is not allowed for jewels and beads, but allowed for small pearls sold by weight.
Ruling is derived from the commentary stating 'لا يجوز' and 'يجوز'.
2
Extract the 'Illa (Effective Cause)
Find the reason for the prohibition. The commentary mentions 'تفاوت آحادها تفاوتاً فاحشاً' (gross disparity of individuals). This is the 'illa that makes the contract invalid.
Gross disparity leads to uncertainty (gharar fahish) which is prohibited.
3
Apply the Exception
Identify why small pearls are excepted. They are sold by weight for kohl and medicine, making them uniform and known. The disparity is removed.
Standardization by weight removes the gharar.
4
Generalize the Principle
Formulate a general rule: salam is invalid in any commodity where individual units are not substantially uniform, but valid if they can be standardized (e.g., by weight, measure, count).
This aligns with the Hanafi condition that the subject matter must be known to a degree that avoids dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواهر، والخَرَز، وبصاع وذراع معين لم يدر قدره
[٣] قوله : والجواهر والخرز ; أي لا يجوزُ السلم في الجواهر كالياقوت والفيروزج، ولا يجوزُ في الخَرَز ، ، وهو الذي ينظم ، وخرزات الملكِ جواهر تاجه، وكان إذا ملك عاماً زيدت في تاجه خرزة ليعلم عدد سني ملكه، قال الجَوْهَرِي : وذلك كالعقيق والبلور، ووجه عدم جواز السَّلَم فيها تفاوت آحادها تفاوتاً فاحشاً، وكذا لا يجوز في اللآلئ الكبار، كذا في «النهر».
وأما في صغار اللآلئ التي تباعُ وزناً للكحل والتداوي فيجوزُ السَّلم فيها ؛ لأنها مما يصير معلوماً، فلا تتفاوت في المالية.
And jewels, and beads, and by a sa' and a specific cubit whose measure is unknown. [3] His statement: 'and jewels and beads'; meaning salam is not allowed in jewels like ruby and turquoise, and not allowed in beads, which are strung. And the king's beads are the jewels of his crown, and if he ruled a year, a bead was added to his crown to know the number of years of his reign. Al-Jawhari said: that is like agate and crystal. And the reason for the non-permissibility of salam in them is the gross disparity of their individuals. Likewise, it is not allowed in large pearls. Thus in al-Nahr. As for small pearls that are sold by weight for kohl and medicine, salam in them is allowed because they become known and do not differ in value.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on salam in jewels (jawahir) and beads (kharaz). The general rule is that salam is not valid for precious stones (ruby, turquoise) and large beads/pearls because each piece is unique and varies greatly in value, leading to gross disparity and potential dispute. The text cites al-Nahr for the same ruling on large pearls. However, a crucial exception is made: small pearls that are sold by weight (for medicinal or cosmetic use) are sufficiently uniform, so salam in them is permissible. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that if the source of disparity is removed (by standardization through weight), the contract becomes valid.

Key Terms
الجواهر (al-jawahir): Precious stones like ruby and turquoise.
الخرز (al-kharaz): Beads (like agate, crystal) used for ornamentation.
اللآلئ الكبار (al-la'ali al-kibar): Large pearls, unique and highly variable in value.
صغار اللآلئ (sighar al-la'ali): Small pearls sold by weight for kohl or medicine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Small pearls are cheaper and easier to find
B) Small pearls are sold by weight and do not vary much in value
C) Large pearls are considered a luxury item and thus excluded by law
D) The Prophet (ﷺ) specifically prohibited large pearls in salam

Key Takeaway
Salam is invalid in items with gross individual variation (jewels, large beads/pearls) but valid if standardized by weight/measure as this removes uncertainty.

Ruling on Salam with an Unknown Measure
Prohibits salam with unknown specific measure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on salam when the measure is a specific but unknown container, and explain the rationale based on avoidance of dispute.

Core Idea
Salam is invalid if the measure is specified but its exact capacity is unknown, because uncertainty leads to dispute.

This lesson examines a key condition in salam (forward sale): the measure of the goods must be precisely known. The text rules that using a specific container (e.g., a particular sa') whose capacity is not known to both parties invalidates the contract. The reason given is the risk of loss or disagreement, which salam must avoid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and condition
Recognize that the passage discusses salam (forward sale) with a specific measure (sa' or dhira') that is unknown in capacity.
Salam is a sale of goods to be delivered in the future, requiring exact specification.
2
State the ruling
The salam is invalid (la yajuz). The measure being specific but unknown makes the contract fasid.
This is a clear prohibition in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the rationale (talil)
The text gives the reason: 'because it could be lost and lead to dispute.' Uncertainty about the exact amount owed creates potential conflict, which salam aims to prevent.
Contrast with a regular sale (bay') where immediate possession avoids this risk.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
Consider a salam where the buyer says 'I will pay you 100 dirhams for this sa' of wheat' pointing to a specific container but neither knows its capacity. The contract is invalid.
If the container's capacity were known (e.g., 1 sa' standard), the salam would be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبصاع وذراع معين لم يدر قدره

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on salam where the measure (sa' or dhira') is a specific container or tool, but its exact capacity is unknown. For example, agreeing to deliver 'a sa' of wheat using this particular container' when the container's volume is not known to both parties. Salam is invalid because if the container is lost or disputed, the exact obligation becomes uncertain, leading to potential conflict. This contrasts with a regular sale (bay') where immediate possession avoids this risk. The ruling underscores the importance of precise, known measures in salam contracts.

Key Terms
الصاع (al-sa'): A specific measuring container for grain; its capacity must be known.
الذراع (al-dhira'): A cubit, used for measuring length; if specified but unknown length, invalid.

Ruling on Salam in Animal Skins, Firewood, and Fresh Branches
States ruling and exceptions for salam in count-based items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on salam in animal skins, firewood, and fresh branches, and explain how specifying type or length can make the contract permissible.

Core Idea
Salam in items sold by count is invalid unless inherent variation is removed by specifying type or length.

In salam contracts, precise specification of the subject matter is essential to avoid dispute. This lesson examines why the Hanafi school invalidates salam in animal skins, firewood, and fresh branches, and how specifying type or length can resolve the uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
The passage discusses salam in three items: animal skins (sold by count), firewood (sold in bundles), and fresh branches (sold in bunches). Note that each is sold by customary unit, not by weight or measure.
Custom (urf) plays a key role in determining how items are sold.
2
Understand the general ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that salam is invalid in these items because they inherently vary in size and quality (tafawut), leading to potential dispute (manaza'a). The ruling is based on the principle of preventing gharar.
Invalid means the contract is fasid (void).
3
Examine the exceptions
Specification can remove the uncertainty. For firewood, specifying the length of what ties the bundle makes it permissible. For skins, specifying a known type (e.g., cow hide, goat skin) makes it permissible according to al-Dhakhira and al-Inaya.
These exceptions show that the core concern is precise specification, not the item itself.
4
Follow the Maliki dispute
Imam Malik permits salam in skins if they are described by weight and description. The Hanafi response: skins are not customarily weighed, so specifying weight does not remove the variation inherent in selling by count.
This illustrates how custom determines the method of specification.
5
Derive the operative principle
The underlying principle is that salam requires the subject matter to be known in a way that prevents dispute, and the method of specification must align with customary practice (urf).
This principle applies to other items sold by count or with inherent variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجلوده عدداً، والحطب حُزَماً ، والرَّطْبَةِ جُرَزْاً. الحزم : جمع الحزمة، وهي بالفارسية «بندهيزم». والجرز : جمع الجرزة، وهي بالفارسية «دسته تره». وإنما لا يجوز في الحطب للتَّفاوت حتى إن بَيَّنَ طول ما يُشَدُّ به الحزمة يجوز. قوله : وجلوده ; أي لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ في جلودِ الحيوان، وقال في «النهاية»: قال مالك له : يجوز؛ لأنه مقدورُ التسليم، معلوم المقدار بالوزن والصفة، ولكنا نقول : الجلود لا توزن عادةً ، فلم يجز وزناً بالطريق الأولى، ولكنها تباع عدداً، متفاوت فيها الصغير والكبير، فلا يجوزُ السَّلّم فيها ؛ لأنه مفض إلى المنازعة. انتهى. وفي «الذخيرة»: إنَّ بيَّن للجلود ضرباً معلوماً يجوز؛ لانتفاء المنازعة، كذا في «العناية».
And its skins by number, and firewood in bundles, and fresh branches in bunches. Al-hazm: plural of hizma, which in Persian is 'bandehizam'. And al-jurz: plural of jurza, which in Persian is 'dasta tarah'. And it is not permissible in firewood due to variation, unless he specifies the length of what the bundle is tied with, then it is permissible. He said: his statement: 'And its skins' means: salam is not permissible in the skins of animals, and it is said in al-Nihaya: Malik said: it is permissible because it is possible to deliver, known in quantity by weight and description, but we say: skins are not customarily weighed, so it is not permissible by weight as a primary route, but they are sold by number, with variation in small and large, so salam is not permissible in them because it leads to dispute. End. And in al-Dhakhira: if he specifies a known type of skins, it is permissible, due to absence of dispute, as in al-Inaya.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on salam in animal skins, firewood, and fresh branches. The rule is that salam is invalid in these items because they are sold by count and vary greatly in size and quality, leading to inevitable dispute. The Hanafi school holds that salam requires precise specification (ma'lum) to avoid uncertainty (gharar). Unlike items sold by weight or measure, skins are not customarily weighed, so specifying by weight does not remove the uncertainty. If the seller specifies a known type of skin (e.g., cowhide vs. goat), then salam becomes permissible as per al-Dhakhira. Similarly, firewood is invalid unless the length of each bundle is specified. The text also notes that Imam Malik allowed salam in skins because they can be described by weight and description. The Hanafi response is that skins are not customarily weighed, so that method does not apply. The ruling is based on the principle of preventing dispute.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): An advance payment sale where the price is paid immediately for deferred delivery of specified goods.
الجلود (al-julud): Animal skins; in this context, items sold by count, not by weight.
تفاوت (tafawut): Variation in size, quality, or value that prevents precise specification and leads to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the Qur'an prohibits salam in skins.
D) Because the price of skins is always uncertain.

Key Takeaway
Salam in items sold by count is invalid unless the inherent variation is removed by specifying type or length, based on the principle of preventing dispute.

Salam in Jewels, Beads, and Unknown Measures
Rules on salam validity in jewels, beads, and unknown measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid salam and identify which items of varying value or unknown measure render salam fasid.

Core Idea
Salam requires certainty: excessive variation or unknown measures invalidate the contract.

In this passage, the author explains why salam (forward sale) is not valid for precious jewels, beads, and when a specific but unknown measure is used. The key reason is that salam involves deferred delivery; if the subject matter varies greatly or the measure is uncertain, it leads to dispute and risk (gharar). However, items like small pearls sold by weight, which are uniform in value, are permissible. This lesson explores the conditions that must be met for a valid salam contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruled items and their hukm
List the items mentioned: jewels, beads, large pearls, small pearls, unknown sa', unknown dhira'. For each, determine if salam is permissible (sahih) or not (fasid) according to the passage.
Small pearls sold by weight are an exception; they are permissible.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
For jewels/beads/large pearls, the reason is excessive variation (tafawut fahish) among individual items. For unknown measures, the reason is that the measure might be lost, leading to dispute. For small pearls, uniformity by weight removes the variation.
The reasoning is based on the principle of avoiding gharar (uncertainty).
3
Distinguish between deferred and spot delivery
The passage notes that a spot sale (bay' hāl) with an unknown measure is valid because delivery is immediate, so the risk of losing the measure is absent. In salam, delivery is delayed, making the uncertainty problematic.
This shows how timing affects the ruling.
4
Apply the principle to other items
Consider other items that might vary, such as types of cloth, grains, or animals. Use the same reasoning: if the items are sold by uniform measure/weight and not subject to excessive variation, salam may be valid.
Always check if the item can be precisely specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواهر، والخَرَز، وبصاع وذراع معين لم يدر قدره. قوله : والجواهر والخرز ; أي لا يجوزُ السلم في الجواهر كالياقوت والفيروزج، ولا يجوزُ في الخَرَز، وهو الذي ينظم، وخرزات الملكِ جواهر تاجه، وكان إذا ملك عاماً زيدت في تاجه خرزة ليعلم عدد سني ملكه، قال الجَوْهَرِي : وذلك كالعقيق والبلور، ووجه عدم جواز السَّلَم فيها تفاوت آحادها تفاوتاً فاحشاً، وكذا لا يجوز في اللآلئ الكبار، كذا في «النهر». وأما في صغار اللآلئ التي تباعُ وزناً للكحل والتداوي فيجوزُ السَّلم فيها ; لأنها مما يصير معلوماً، فلا تتفاوت في المالية. قوله : وبصاع وذراع ...الخ ; أي لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ بصاع معيَّن وذراع معين لا يدرى قدر ذلك الصاع والذراع ; لأنه يحتمل أن يضيع فيؤدي إلى النزاع بخلاف البيع حالاً حيث يجوز; لأنَّ التسليم به يجب في الحال، فلا يتوهم فوته، وفي السَّلَم بتأخير التسليم فيخاف فوتُه.
And jewels, and beads, and a specific sa' and a specific dhira' whose measure is not known. He said: his statement: 'And jewels and beads' means: salam is not permissible in jewels like rubies and turquoise, and it is not permissible in beads, which are strung, and the beads of the king are the jewels of his crown; whenever he ruled a year, a bead was added to his crown to know the number of years of his reign. Al-Jawhari said: that is like cornelian and crystal. And the reason for the impermissibility of salam in them is the excessive variation among individuals. Similarly, it is not permissible in large pearls, as in al-Nahr. As for small pearls which are sold by weight for kohl and medicine, salam in them is permissible because they become known and do not vary in value. He said: his statement: 'And a specific sa' and dhira'' means: salam is not permissible with a specific sa' and a specific dhira' whose measure is not known, because it is possible that it may be lost and lead to dispute, unlike a sale on the spot where it is permissible, because delivery is required immediately, so its loss is not feared, while in salam delivery is delayed and its loss is feared.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage addresses salam in jewels, beads, and with specific but unknown measures. Jewels like rubies and turquoise vary greatly in value, making salam invalid. Beads also vary, even those used historically for counting. Large pearls are included. However, small pearls sold by weight for medicinal purposes are permissible because they are uniform in value. This distinction is based on the principle that salam requires the subject matter to be known (ma'lum) and not subject to excessive variation (tafawut fahish). Additionally, salam is invalid when the measure itself is unknown, e.g., a specific sa' or dhira' whose exact capacity is unknown, because it may be lost or forgotten, leading to dispute. In contrast, a spot sale is valid even with such a measure because delivery is immediate, so the risk of loss is absent. This highlights the importance of certainty in salam's deferred delivery.

Key Terms
الجواهر (al-jawahir): Precious stones like rubies and turquoise; items with extreme variation in value.
الخرز (al-kharaz): Beads used for stringing; also vary in value.
صاع وذراع معين (sa' wa dhira' mu'ayyan): A specific measuring cup or cubit whose exact capacity/length is unknown; not permissible for salam.

Salam in Specific Source vs. Region
States ruling on salam with specific source vs. region/quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam when the subject is tied to a specific source and distinguish this from salam based on regional or qualitative attribution.

Core Idea
Salam in a specific village's wheat or a specific tree's fruit is invalid due to risk of total loss; salam tied to a region or quality is valid.

In salam (forward sale), the subject matter must be deliverable. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains when a salam contract becomes invalid because the specified source is too narrow. Understanding the distinction between source specification and quality description is crucial for valid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is salam (forward sale) where the subject is specified as wheat from a particular village or fruit from a particular tree. The ruling is that this salam is fasid (invalid).
The ruling is derived from the risk that the specific source could be destroyed before delivery.
2
Locate the illa (legal reason)
The illa is the possibility of an affliction (blight, drought) that could destroy the entire source, making delivery impossible.
This is based on practicality: if delivery is uncertain, the contract is invalid.
3
Distinguish the valid case
Salam tied to a large region (e.g., a province like Iraq) is valid because the risk of total loss is rare. Similarly, if the specification is for quality description (like a well-known variety), it is valid because the intention is the type, not the specific crop.
The examples given: al-Khashmarani (a variety from Bukhara) and al-Basakhi (from Farghana) are permissible.
4
Apply the distinction
When analyzing a salam contract, determine whether the source specification is for the actual crop (invalid) or for quality description (valid).
Custom (urf) plays a role: if the village name is commonly used as a quality indicator, it is treated as such.
5
Note the practical impact
This rule ensures that salam contracts are enforceable and not speculative. The burden is on the contracting parties to describe the subject in a way that guarantees deliverability.
For further detail, the text refers to al-Fath (likely al-Fath al-Qadir).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وبر قرية وثمرة نخلة متعينتين; أي لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ فيهما ; لاحتمال أن يعتبر بهما آفة فينقطعا عن أيدي الناس، فلا يقدر على تسليمهما، ولو أسلم في بُر ولاية يجوز; لأنَّ وصول الآفة لبرِّ كل الولاية نادر، وهذا إذا كانت النسبة إلى قرية ليؤدي من بُرها. وإذا كانت النسبة إلى قريةٍ معينةٍ لبيان الصفة لا لتعيين الخارج من أرضها بعينه، كالخَشْمَرَانِي ببخارا والبَسَاخي : وهي قرية حنطتها جيدة بفَرغانة، لا بأس به ; لأنه لا يراد خصوص الثابت هناك، بل الإقليم، ولا يتوهم انقطاع طعام إقليم بكماله، فالسَّلَمُ فيه وفي طعام العراق والشام سواء، وكذا في ديار مصر في قمح الصعيد، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى «الفتح».
He said: his statement: 'And wheat of a specific village and fruit of a specific palm tree' means: salam is not permissible in them due to the possibility that an affliction may befall them and they become unavailable, so he cannot deliver them. If he makes salam in wheat of a whole province, it is permissible because affliction to all the wheat of the province is rare. This applies when the attribution to the village is to deliver from its wheat. If the attribution to a specific village is to describe the quality, not to designate the actual produce of that land—like al-Khashmarani in Bukhara and al-Basakhi, which is a village in Farghana known for good wheat—then there is no harm, because the intention is not the specific produce there but the region, and it is not feared that the food supply of an entire region will be cut off. So salam in that is the same as salam in the food of Iraq and Syria, and likewise in Egypt in the wheat of the Sa'id. If you want further detail, refer to al-Fath.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses salam when the subject matter is specifically tied to a particular source. Salam is invalid if the wheat is specified as coming from a particular village or the fruit from a particular tree, because a blight could destroy that entire source, making delivery impossible. However, if the attribution is to a province (like Bukhara) or a region (like Iraq), it is valid because it's unlikely that all wheat in that large area will be destroyed. Similarly, attributing to a village for quality description (like a specific well-known wheat variety) is valid as long as the intention is the type, not the actual crop from that particular land. The commentary gives examples: al-Khashmarani (a variety from Bukhara) and al-Basakhi (from Farghana) are permissible because they denote quality, not a specific batch. The same ruling applies to wheat of the Sa'id in Egypt. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that salam is invalid when the subject is susceptible to total loss, but valid when the source is general enough to ensure availability.

Key Terms
بر قرية معينة (bur qarya mu'ayyana): Wheat attributed to a specific village; salam is invalid due to risk of total loss.
ثمر نخلة معينة (thamar nakhla mu'ayyana): Fruit of a specific palm tree; similarly invalid.
الإقليم (al-iqlīm): A larger region (e.g., Iraq, Syria); salam tied to a region is valid because total loss is rare.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because provincial wheat is always cheaper.
B) Because the risk of total loss is rare for a whole province.
C) Because village wheat is of lower quality.
D) Because salam is only allowed for imported goods.

Key Takeaway
Salam is invalid when the subject is tied to a specific source vulnerable to total loss; valid when tied to a region or quality description.

Conditions of Salam Commodity Existence — Hanafi Rulings
Rules on salam commodity existence from contract to delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios of salam commodity existence and apply the Hanafi ruling (sahih/fasid) to each case, including the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Salam is valid only if the commodity exists from contract to delivery; any interruption invalidates the contract.

In a salam contract, the commodity (muslam fih) is delivered later, so its continued existence is crucial to avoid gharar (uncertainty). The Hanafi school sets strict conditions: the commodity must be available at both the time of contract and at delivery, without an intervening period of unavailability. This lesson explains the four possible scenarios and the point of disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
List the four scenarios
Read the four cases described in the passage: (1) available at contract, unavailable at delivery; (2) unavailable at contract, available at delivery; (3) available at both but unavailable in between; (4) available throughout.
Ensure you understand the timeline: contract time (time of sale) and delivery time (when the salam commodity is due).
2
Identify the consensus cases
Scenarios 1 and 4 are ruled by consensus (ijma'). Scenario 1 is invalid because delivery cannot be guaranteed; scenario 4 is valid because full existence is ensured.
Consensus includes all major schools, not just Hanafi.
3
Analyze the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute
Scenario 3: commodity available at both ends but unavailable in between. Hanafi says invalid due to the period of uncertainty; Shafi'i says valid because delivery ability at maturity is what matters.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that the commodity must be continuously existent to avoid gharar.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule to scenario 2
Though partially missing from the text, scenario 2 (unavailable at contract, available later) is also invalid according to Hanafi because existence at contract is a necessary condition.
Hanafi requires existence at contract to ensure that the seller can realistically deliver later.
5
Summarize the practical effect
Only scenario 4 (continuous existence) yields a valid salam in Hanafi. All other scenarios render the contract fasid (voidable). In Shafi'i, scenario 3 is valid.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that in scenario 3, the contract is either fasid (Hanafi) or valid (Shafi'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفيما لم يوجد .. الخ ; أي لا يجوزُ السَّلَمُ فيما لا يوجد من حين العقدِ إلى حين المحل هذه المسألة على وجوه أربعة : ١ . إن كان المسلم فيه موجوداً عند العقد، منقطعاً عن أيدي الناس عند حلول الأجل لا يصح اتفاقاً. ٢. وإن كان منقطعاً وقت العقد موجوداً في أيدي الناس عند المحل. . أو كان موجوداً عند العقد وعند المحل، ومنقطعاً فيما بينهما لا يصح عندنا خلافاً للشافعي٤. وإن كان موجوداً من وقت العقد إلى وقت المحل يصح اتفاقاً.
He said: his statement: 'And regarding that which is not found...' means: salam is not permissible in that which is not found from the time of contract until the time of harvest. This issue has four aspects: 1. If the subject matter is available at the time of contract but becomes unavailable at the time of delivery, it is invalid by consensus. 2. If it is unavailable at contract but available at delivery... [or] it is available at both contract and delivery but unavailable in between, it is invalid according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. 4. If it is available from the time of contract until the time of delivery, it is valid by consensus.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of the salam commodity's existence relative to contract and delivery periods. There are four scenarios: (1) Available at contract but unavailable at delivery: invalid by consensus (ijma'). (2) Unavailable at contract but available at delivery: the text seems to have a gap; based on typical Hanafi rulings, this is also invalid because salam requires existence at contract to ensure eventual delivery. (3) Available at both contract and delivery but unavailable in between: Hanafi says invalid because there is a period where it was unavailable, leading to uncertainty; al-Shafi'i says valid because it is available at delivery, which is when delivery is due. (4) Available throughout: valid by consensus. The Hanafi position emphasizes that the commodity must be continuously available or at least expected to be available at delivery without interruption, to avoid gharar. The dispute with al-Shafi'i highlights the different thresholds for certainty: Shafi'i focuses on deliverability at the time of delivery, while Hanafi requires stability.

Key Terms
مُسلم فيه (muslam fih): The subject matter of a salam contract; its existence conditions are critical for validity.
حلول الأجل (hulul al-ajal): The time when delivery is due; availability at that time is necessary.
منقطعاً (munqati'an): Unavailable, not present in the hands of people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if the salam commodity is available at the time of contract and at delivery but unavailable in between?
A) Valid by consensus.
B) Invalid according to Hanafi, valid according to Shafi'i.
C) Invalid according to all schools.
D) Valid only if the seller compensates for the unavailability.

Key Takeaway
Salam contracts are valid only if the commodity exists continuously from contract to delivery; any interruption renders the contract fasid in the Hanafi school.

Conditions for Existence of Muslam Fihi in Salam
Explains ruling on salam with specified origin and four cases of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for the continuous existence of the muslam fihi in a salam contract and apply them to the four scenarios of availability.

Core Idea
Salam requires the commodity to exist from contract to delivery — missing at any point invalidates the contract.

Salam is a forward sale where the price is paid upfront and goods delivered later. Its validity hinges on the availability of the subject matter. In this passage, the commentator lays out four scenarios and explains when a salam contract is valid or invalid, citing the Prophetic hadith. Understanding these conditions is crucial for structuring valid salam contracts in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special cases
Note that salam in wheat of a specific village or fruit of a specific palm tree is invalid because a blight could make delivery impossible. However, if the attribution is to describe quality (e.g., regional wheat), it is valid.
The key is whether the source is so limited that total loss is probable.
2
Understand the four scenarios of existence
Review the four cases: (1) exists at contract but not at delivery → invalid by consensus; (2) missing at contract but present at delivery → invalid in Hanafi; (3) present at both ends but missing in between → invalid in Hanafi; (4) present throughout → valid by consensus.
Hanafis require continuous existence; Shafi'i differs on cases 2 and 3.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith 'Do not make salam in fruits until their ripeness becomes apparent' shows that existence at contract is required. Also, salam resembles a contract of the insolvent (selling what one does not have), so continuous existence minimizes risk.
The hadith is cited from Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah.
4
Note the Shafi'i disagreement and Hanafi response
Shafi'i argues that ability to deliver at delivery is sufficient. Hanafis respond that the hadith's restriction proves otherwise, and the probability of non-delivery (e.g., seller's death) makes continuous existence necessary.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Shafi'i, a contract for unharvested wheat could be valid; under Hanafi, it is invalid.

[ ] قوله : قوله ... الخ ؛ أخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجة، واللفظ له)، وأخرج البخاري عن البختري قال : سألت ابن عمر له عن السلم في النخل، فقال: «نهى رسول الله عن بيع النخل حتى يصلح، وعن بيع الورق نسيئاً بناجز» (۳)، وسألت ابن عباس عن السلم في النخل، فقال: «نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن بيع النخل حتى تؤكل منه، كذا في «نصب الراية»(4). [٢]قوله : لا تسلموا... الخ ؛ الحديث دلَّ على أنَّ القدرة عند المحل غير كافية لجواز العقد، إذ لو كان لم يكن لتقييد النَّبي الله بقوله : «حتى يبدو صلاحها» فائدة، وعلى أنَّ الوجود معتبر من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، كذا في «الكفاية»). (۳) قوله : ولأنه ... الخ ؛ ولاحتمال موت المسلّم إليه بعد العقد قبل أن يبلغ المحل، إذ يحل الأجلُ ويلزم التسليم، والاحتمال في هذا العقدِ ملحق بالحقيقة، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر» (1). . [٤] قوله : فلا بد...الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ المسلَّمَ فيه وإن وجد عند المحل لكن من الجائز أن لا يقدر المسلم إليه على اكتسابه حينئذ، فيشترط الوجود في جملة المدة، حتى لو لم يقدر بعض الزمان يقدر في البعض الآخر، كذا في حواشي «الهداية»).
[1] And wheat of a specific village and fruit of a specific palm tree, and regarding what does not exist from the time of contract until the time of delivery. According to al-Shafi'i, it is permissible if it exists at the time of delivery because of ability to deliver at its time of existence. Immediately, it is permissible because delivery is obligated immediately, so its loss is not feared; but in salam, delivery is delayed, so its loss is feared. [1] His statement: 'wheat of a specific village and fruit of a specific palm tree' means salam in them is not permissible due to the possibility that a blight may affect them and they may be cut off from people's hands, so one cannot deliver them. If one makes salam in wheat of a region, it is permissible because the arrival of blight to all wheat of the region is rare. This is when the attribution to a village is to require delivery from its wheat. When the attribution to a specific village is to describe the quality, not to specifically designate the product from its land—like al-Khashmarani from Bukhara and al-Basakhi, which is a village with good wheat in Farghana—there is no harm, because what is intended is not the particular produce there but the region, and the complete food supply of a region is not imagined to be cut off. So salam in it and in the food of Iraq and Syria is the same, and likewise in the land of Egypt in the wheat of Upper Egypt. If you want further detail, refer to al-Fath. [2] His statement: 'Regarding what does not exist...' means salam is not permissible in what does not exist from the time of contract until the time of delivery. This issue has four aspects: 1. If the muslam fihi exists at the time of contract but is cut off from people's hands at the time of delivery, it is not valid by agreement. 2. If it is cut off at the time of contract but exists in people's hands at delivery, or exists at both contract and delivery but is cut off in between, it is not valid with us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. 4. If it exists from the time of contract to the time of delivery, it is valid by agreement. Our proof: His statement: 'Do not make salam in fruits until their ripeness becomes apparent,' and because it is a contract of the insolvent, so continuity of existence throughout the term is necessary for fulfillment. The limit of discontinuity is that it is not found in the market where it is sold, even if it is found in houses. Thus in al-Dhakhira, mentioned by al-Kirmani in al-Kifaya. [ ] His statement: His statement... etc. Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah narrated it, with their wording. Al-Bukhari narrated from al-Bakhtari who said: I asked Ibn 'Umar about salam in palm trees, and he said: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade selling palm trees until they become good, and selling silver on credit for cash.' And I asked Ibn 'Abbas about salam in palm trees, and he said: 'The Prophet forbade selling palm trees until they are eaten from.' Thus in Nasb al-Raya. [2] His statement: 'Do not make salam...' The hadith indicates that ability at delivery is not sufficient for the permissibility of the contract; for if it were, the Prophet's restriction 'until its ripeness becomes apparent' would have no benefit. And it indicates that existence is considered from the time of contract to the time of delivery. Thus in al-Kifaya. (3) His statement: 'Because it is a contract of the insolvent...' And because of the possibility of the seller's death after the contract before delivery time, when the term comes and delivery is obligatory; probability in this contract is treated as reality. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. [4] His statement: 'It is necessary...' means that even if the muslam fihi exists at delivery, it may be that the seller cannot acquire it at that time, so existence throughout the period is required, so that if he cannot at one time, he can at another. Thus in Hashiyat al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya discusses the conditions for the validity of a salam contract regarding the existence of the subject matter (muslam fihi). The author first addresses two special cases: salam in wheat specifically from a named village or fruit from a named palm tree. This is invalid because a blight could wipe out that specific crop, making delivery impossible. However, if the attribution to a village is merely to describe quality (like a well-known wheat variety), it is permissible because the region's entire supply is unlikely to disappear. Then the text presents four scenarios for the existence of the muslam fihi from contract time to delivery time. The Hanafi position is that the commodity must be available throughout the entire period; if it is missing at any point (except possibly at the start if available later?), the contract is invalid. The specific position: (1) exists at contract but not at delivery → invalid by consensus; (2) missing at contract but present at delivery → invalid according to Hanafis; (3) present at both but missing in between → invalid according to Hanafis; (4) present throughout → valid by consensus. The Shafi'i school disagrees on cases 2 and 3, allowing salam if the commodity is available at delivery. The Hanafi proof is the hadith prohibiting salam in fruits until ripeness, which shows that future availability alone is insufficient—the existence must be from the start. Another proof is that salam is a contract of the insolvent (i.e., the seller does not have the goods at contract), so there is a risk of non-delivery; requiring continuous existence minimizes that risk. The discontinuity is defined as the commodity not being found in the market where it is sold, even if it exists in private homes. The commentary also notes that the reasoning includes the possibility of the seller's death before delivery, which would make delivery impossible, so continuous existence provides the seller with opportunity to acquire.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid immediately and the commodity is delivered later.
المسلم فيه (al-muslam fihi): The commodity being sold in a salam contract, which must be precisely described and available for delivery.
حين المحل (hin al-mahall): The time when delivery of the muslam fihi is due, i.e., the end of the term.
الانقطاع (al-inqita'): Discontinuity of availability: the commodity is not found in the market where it is sold, even if it exists in private homes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a person makes salam in wheat that is available at the time of contract but becomes scarce (not found in the market) at the time of delivery?
A) The contract is valid because it was available at contract.
B) The contract is invalid because it was not available at delivery.
C) The contract is valid by consensus.
D) The contract is invalid only according to al-Shafi'i.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the muslam fihi must exist continuously from contract to delivery for the salam contract to be valid.

Continuous Existence of the Subject Matter in Salam
Hanafi proof that salam commodity must exist continuously from contract to delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of continuous existence in salam, derive it from the hadith, and apply it to determine contract validity.

Core Idea
The muslam fihi must exist continuously from contract to delivery; existence at term alone is insufficient.

Salam is a forward sale where the seller does not possess the goods at the time of contract. The Hanafi school requires that the commodity exist continuously throughout the term, not just at delivery. This lesson examines the textual and rational proofs for this condition, and how it is applied in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a salam contract. The ruling is that the commodity (muslam fihi) must exist continuously from the time of contract to the time of delivery. If existence discontinues, the contract is fasid.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Do not make salam in fruits until their ripeness becomes apparent.' This is a sunnah ahad, zanni al-thubut but with clear meaning.
The hadith is cited in al-Kifayah and al-Dhakhira.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative prohibition 'la tusallimu' indicates the contract is invalid if the condition is not met. The phrase 'hatta yabdu salahu' shows that existence is required from the start, not just at maturity.
If ability at term were enough, the Prophet's restriction would have no benefit.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school considers existence at the term sufficient. The Hanafis argue that because salam is a contract of the absent (aqd al-mafalis), continuous existence is necessary to ensure delivery.
The possibility of the seller's death or inability to acquire the goods at term further supports the Hanafi view.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that continuous existence is required. If the commodity is unavailable in the public market during the term, even if available privately, the contract becomes fasid.
This is affirmed by al-Kirmani in al-Kifayah and later works like Majma' al-Anhur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا تسلموا في الثَّمار حتى يبدو صلاحها) ، ولأنه عقد المفاليس فلا بد من استمرار الوجود في مدة الأجل ليتمكن من التحصيل. وحد الانقطاع : هو أن لا يوجد في السُّوقِ الذي يباع فيه، وإن كان يوجد في البيوت. كذا في «الذخيرة»، ذكر العلامةُ الكِرْماني في «الكفاية»). الحديث دلَّ على أنَّ القدرة عند المحل غير كافية لجواز العقد، إذ لو كان لم يكن لتقييد النَّبي بقوله : «حتى يبدو صلاحها» فائدة، وعلى أنَّ الوجود معتبر من حين العقد إلى حين المحل، كذا في «الكفاية». ولأنه عقد المفاليس فلا بد من استمرار الوجود في مدة الأجل ليتمكن من التحصيل. وحد الانقطاع : هو أن لا يوجد في السُّوقِ الذي يباع فيه، وإن كان يوجد في البيوت. كذا في «الذخيرة» ولاحتمال موت المسلّم إليه بعد العقد قبل أن يبلغ المحل، إذ يحل الأجلُ ويلزم التسليم، والاحتمال في هذا العقدِ ملحق بالحقيقة، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». يعني أنَّ المسلَّمَ فيه وإن وجد عند المحل لكن من الجائز أن لا يقدر المسلم إليه على اكتسابه حينئذ، فيشترط الوجود في جملة المدة، حتى لو لم يقدر بعض الزمان يقدر في البعض الآخر، كذا في حواشي «الهداية».
He said: (Do not make salam in fruits until their ripeness becomes apparent), and because it is a contract of the absent, so continuity of existence during the term is necessary for him to be able to acquire it. And the limit of discontinuation is that it is not available in the market where it is sold, even if it is available in homes. This is in al-Dhakhira, as stated by al-Kirmani in al-Kifayah. The hadith indicates that ability at the time of maturity is insufficient for the validity of the contract; otherwise there would be no benefit in the Prophet's (SAW) restriction by his saying 'until its ripeness becomes apparent', and that existence is considered from the time of contract to the time of delivery, as in al-Kifayah. And because it is a contract of the absent, continuity of existence during the term is necessary for him to be able to acquire it. And the limit of discontinuation is that it is not available in the market where it is sold, even if it is available in homes. This is in al-Dhakhira. And due to the possibility of the death of the recipient after the contract before the term arrives, for then the term matures and delivery becomes obligatory, and the possibility in this contract is treated as reality, as in Majma' al-Anhur. It means that the subject matter of salam, even if it exists at the term, it is possible that the recipient may not be able to acquire it at that time, so existence is required throughout the entire period, so that if he cannot at one time, he may be able at another, as in the footnotes of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe on a matn statement regarding the conditions of salam. The commentator begins by citing the Prophet's hadith, 'Do not make salam in fruits until their ripeness becomes apparent,' to establish that the mere potential to deliver at the end of the term is insufficient. Instead, the commodity (muslam fihi) must exist continuously from the time of contract until delivery. This is because salam is a contract for a specified future delivery, and if the commodity could disappear from the market, the seller might fail to deliver. The ruling sets a standard: discontinuation means the item is unavailable in the public market, even if it remains in private homes. The reasoning also considers the possibility of the seller's death before maturity, which would force immediate delivery. Since such possibilities are real in salam, they necessitate continuous existence. Thus, the Hanafi position relies on both textual proof (hadith) and rational reasoning (qiyas based on the nature of the contract).

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for delayed delivery of a specified commodity
عقد المفاليس (aqd al-mafalis): Literally 'contract of the insolvent'; refers to the seller who may not have the goods in his possession at the time of contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only at the time of the contract
B) Only at the time of delivery
C) Continuously from contract to delivery
D) At the time of ripeness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the muslam fihi to exist continuously from contract to delivery; any discontinuation renders the contract fasid.

Salam in Meat: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Conditions of Specification
Ruling on salam in meat and conditions of specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on salam in meat, apply the conditions of jins, naw', sifat, and hadd, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The fatwa allows salam in meat if fully specified, despite Abu Hanifa's objection.

Salam (advance payment for future delivery) in meat presents a classic Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa feared that even with description, meat varies too much, leading to disputes. His two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, held that if the meat is fully described—including its genus, type, condition, location, and quantity—it becomes as predictable as tail fat. Their view became the school's operative position. This lesson unpacks the debate and the exact conditions needed to remove uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
Find the statement that Abu Hanifa does not allow salam in meat. Note the reason: variation in bone size and fatness leads to potential dispute (gharar).
2
Examine the Sahibayn's counter-position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow salam if the meat is described in detail. Identify the five elements they require: jins, naw', sifat, mawdi', and miqdar.
The text gives an example: a castrated, two-year-old, fat sheep from the side, one hundred mann.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi narrations on deboned meat
There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one allowing deboned meat (Hasan ibn Ziyad), one disallowing even that (Ibn Shuja'). Understand the reasoning: variation in fatness.
4
Understand the legal definitions of jins and naw'
The passage clarifies that jins and naw' are used in the legal sense, not logical. Jins includes individuals differing in rulings; naw' includes individuals agreeing in rulings. Example: 'man' is a jins because male and female have different rulings.
This is crucial for correctly specifying the subject of salam.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text states that the fatwa is on the view of the Sahibayn (and the three Imams). This means salam in meat is valid with full specification.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical effect is that such contracts are enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في اللحم ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : يصح إن بَينَ جنسَهُ ونوعه وصفته وموضعَهُ وقدرَهُ كشاةٍ خصيَّةٍ وثني سمين من الجنب مئة من. وشروطه : ۱. بیان جنسيه :: قوله : عند أبي حنيفة أنه يختلف باختلاف كبر العظم وصغرِهِ فيؤدي إلى المنازعة، وفي منزوع اللحم روايتان عن أبي حنيفة : في رواية الحسن عن زيادة : يجوز ، وفي رواية ابن شجاع : لا يجوز ؛ لأنه يختلف بالسمن والهزال. قوله : وقالا ...الخ ؛ وبه قالت الثلاثة، وعليه الفتوى ، كذا في «البحر» ولهما : إنه موزون مضبوط الوصف، فصار كالإلية والشحم ، بخلاف لحم الطيور ؛ فإنّه لا يقدر على وصف مواضعَ منه ، كذا في «الرمز». قوله : وشروطه ؛ والمراد بها الشروط التي تذكر في العقد ؛ لأنَّ للسلم شروط سكت عنها المصنف له ؛ لأنها لم يشترط ذكرها في السلم، بل وجودها فقط، كعدم الخيار، وعدم علّتي الربا وغيرهما. قوله : جنسه ; ليس المراد بالجنس وكذا بالنوع ما اصطلح عليه المنطقيون، بل ما اصطلح عليه الأصوليون والفقهاء، فالجنس عندهم : كلُّ مقول على أفراد مختلفة من حيث المقاصد والأحكام، والنوع عندهم كلي يقالُ على أفراد متفقة من حيث المقاصد والأحكام. فالإنسان مثلاً جنس عند الفقهاء والأصوليين ; لاختلاف أفرادِهِ في المقاصدِ والأحكام، فإنَّ الذكر منه مخلوق لمصالح لم تخلق الأنثى لها، ولكل واحد منهما تفرق...
And not in meat. This is according to Abu Hanifa. But the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is valid if he specifies its genus, type, description, location, and quantity, like a castrated sheep, a two-year-old, fat, from the side, one hundred mann. And its conditions: 1. Clarification of its genus. He said: According to Abu Hanifa, because it varies with the size of the bone and thus leads to dispute. And regarding meat without bone, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: in the narration of al-Hasan from Ziyada: it is valid, and in the narration of Ibn Shuja': it is not valid, because it varies with fatness and thinness. He said: And the two said, etc. And the three (Imams) said this, and upon it is the fatwa, as in al-Bahr. And for them (the two), it is a weighed item whose description is defined, so it becomes like the tail-fat and fat, unlike the meat of birds, because its parts cannot be described, as in al-Ramz. He said: And its conditions – what is meant are the conditions that are mentioned in the contract, because salam has conditions that the author (of the matn) omitted, because he did not require their mention in the contract, only their existence, like the absence of option and the causes of riba and others. He said: His genus – what is meant by genus and likewise type is not what the logicians terminologically defined, but rather what the usuliyyun and fuqaha defined. So genus according to them: every universal predicated on individuals differing in purposes and rulings; and type according to them is a universal predicated on individuals agreeing in purposes and rulings. For example, man is a genus according to the fuqaha and usuliyyun because its individuals differ in purposes and rulings. Indeed, the male is created for interests for which the female was not created, and each of them has a distinct [ruling]...

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe on a matn statement about salam in meat. It begins with the ruling: Abu Hanifa holds that salam in meat is not valid because meat varies with bone size and fatness, leading to disputes. However, two narrations from him exist: one allowing deboned meat (valid according to Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration), and one disallowing even that due to variation in fatness. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow salam in meat if fully specified: genus (e.g., sheep), type (e.g., castrated, two-year-old), description (e.g., fat), location (e.g., from the side), and quantity (e.g., 100 mann). Their view is the basis of fatwa. The analogy is that meat is a weighed, describable item like tail fat. The passage then explains the conditions of salam, specifically the requirement to clarify genus (jins) and type (naw') in a legal, not logical, sense. The legal definitions emphasize purposes and rulings, as illustrated by the example of man: male and female are different in rulings, so man is a genus, and the type would be the specific gender.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): A universal category that includes individuals differing in legal rulings and purposes, e.g., man (includes male and female with different rulings).
نوع (naw'): A universal category that includes individuals agreeing in legal rulings and purposes, e.g., 'sheep' as a type of animal for salam.
شروط السلم (shurut al-salam): Conditions that must be mentioned in the salam contract to remove gharar: clarification of jins, naw', sifat, and miqdar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, is salam in meat valid?
A) No, according to Abu Hanifa's view
B) Yes, if fully specified according to the Sahibayn
C) Yes, but only for deboned meat
D) No, because meat is not a weighed item

Key Takeaway
Salam in meat is valid in the Hanafi school when the meat is fully described to remove uncertainty.
