Container Cannot Serve as Measure in Salam
Ruling that salam commodity must be measured by volume or weight, not by container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that salam commodities must be measured by standard volume or weight, explain why a container is insufficient, and distinguish the Shafi‘i position.

Core Idea
A container is not a valid measure in salam because quantity must be fixed; gharar invalidates.

In salam contracts, the quantity must be precisely known to avoid uncertainty (gharar). This lesson examines a key condition: the measure must be by standard volume (kayl) or weight (wazn), not by a container (zambil) whose capacity varies. We will see the Hanafi proof, the Shafi‘i objection, and the refinement by later Hanafi commentators like ‘Allamah al-Shami.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State the Hanafi rule: salam commodity must be measured by kayl or wazn. The passage begins with 'fala yuja‘alu al-zanbīl kaylan aw waznan' – so do not make the basket a measure. This is a prohibition indicating invalidity if violated.
The rule is conditional: if the commodity is normally measured by volume/weight, that must be used.
2
Grasp the rationale: why container leads to gharar
The text explains that goods like cotton can be compressed or expanded. A container does not fix the quantity because the commodity's volume changes under pressure, leading to potential dispute. This is the Hanafi dalil through qiyas on the principle of avoiding uncertainty.
The key term is 'yanqabid wa yankabis' (contracts and compresses).
3
Understand the Shafi‘i alternative and its proof
Imam al-Shafi‘i permits salam in compressible goods even if measured by container, as long as the commodity remains in its container; he argues the container specifies (yuta‘ayyan) the goods. This is based on the idea that the container itself provides adequate identification.
The text says 'li-annahu ‘inda baqā’i ‘aynihi yata‘ayyan' – because as long as its essence remains, it becomes specified.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response via al-Shami
‘Allamah al-Shami refutes al-Zayla‘i's support for specification. He argues that even if the vessel's capacity is verified, it does not make the commodity determinate; otherwise destroying the vessel would invalidate the contract. The real condition is knowledge of the quantity, which can be achieved separately, not by the container as a measure.
Al-Shami clarifies that al-Hidayah's statement 'la budda' (it is necessary) is not an extra condition but an explanation that the measure must be known.
5
Apply the rule to compressible goods
The text concludes that if the commodity is compressible (yanqabid wa yankabis), it cannot be fixed by a container because the variation leads to dispute. Therefore, salam in such goods is invalid unless the quantity is known by standard measure. This underscores the Hanafi emphasis on precision.
This also explains why immediate sale (bay‘) of such goods is disallowed – the same gharar applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يجعل الزنبيل كيلاً أو وزناً. وأجله معلوماً. هذا عندنا، وأما عند الشافعي له يجوز السلم في الكبس وعدمه؛ لأنه عند بقاء عينه يتعيّن، وقول الزَّيْلَعِي: لا لتعيينه؛ ممنوع، نعم هلاكه بعد العلم بمقداره لا يفسد العقد. انتهى. وقال العلامة الشامي: قلت: ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ لأن الوعاء إذا تحقق رفة قدره لا يتعيَّن قطعاً، وإلا فسد العقد بعد هلاكه، ولا نزاع بعد معرفة قدره؛ لإمكان العدول إلى ما عرف من مقداره، فيسلّمهُ بلا منازعة، كما إذا هلك؛ لأن الكلام فيما عُرف قدره. ويظهر في الجواب عن «الهداية» بأن قوله: ولا بد ... الخ؛ بيان لما يُعْرَفُ قدره لا شرط زائد عليه، ويكون المراد أنه إذا كان مما ينقبض وينكبس بالكبس لا يتقدر بمقدار معين؛ لتفاوت الانقباض والكبس، فيؤدّي إلى النزاع؛ ولذا لم يجز البيع فيه حالاً، فكلام الزَّيْلَعِي له وارد على ما يتبادر من كلام «الهداية» من أنه شرط زائد على معرفة القدر على ما قلنا. انتهى.
So do not make the basket a measure by volume or weight. And its term must be known. This is according to us. As for according to al-Shafi‘i, he permits salam in pressed and unpressed [goods] because as long as its essence remains, it becomes specified (determinate). The statement of al-Zayla‘i: 'No, because of its specification' is untenable. However, its destruction after knowing its quantity does not invalidate the contract. End. And ‘Allamah al-Shami said: I say: What is in it is not hidden; because the vessel, if its capacity is verified, is not necessarily determinate; otherwise the contract would be invalid after its destruction. There is no dispute after knowing its quantity because it is possible to refer to what is known of its quantity; so he delivers it without dispute, as if it were destroyed, because the discussion concerns what is known of its quantity. And it appears in the answer to al-Hidayah that his statement 'it is necessary' etc. is an explanation of what is known of its quantity, not an extra condition. The meaning is that if it is something that expands and compresses with pressing, it cannot be determined by a fixed measure because of the variation in expansion and compression, leading to dispute; therefore, sale is not permitted in it immediately. So al-Zayla‘i's statement applies to what is understood from al-Hidayah that it is an extra condition beyond knowing the quantity, as we have said. End.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute clarification. The main rule: in a salam contract, the commodity must be measured by standard volume (kayl) or weight (wazn). A container like a zambil (basket) is not a valid measure because it is indeterminate; its capacity varies with compression. The author states our Hanafi position: this is required. Then he contrasts with al-Shafi‘i who permits salam even in compressible goods (like cotton) because as long as the commodity remains in its container, it becomes determinate (by specification). The Hanafi rebuttal: al-Zayla‘i's argument that specification prevents uncertainty is rejected; rather, if the quantity is known, the container is not the measure. After knowing the quantity, the contract is valid. Then ‘Allamah al-Shami refines: if the vessel's capacity is verified, it does not necessarily make the commodity determinate; otherwise the contract would be invalid upon destruction of the container. Since the quantity can be known separately, the container is not a required condition. The answer to al-Hidayah clarifies that the condition 'la budda' (it is necessary) is not an extra condition but an explanation that the measure must be known. If the commodity is compressible, it cannot be fixed, so immediate sale is invalid. al-Zayla‘i's criticism is based on a misunderstanding of al-Hidayah as imposing an extra condition.

Key Terms
الزنبيل (al-zanbīl): A basket or container used to carry goods; in this context, a measure by container rather than by standard volume or weight.
الكيل (al-kayl): Measurement by volume, a standard method to determine quantity.
الوزن (al-wazn): Measurement by weight, another standard method.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is measuring the commodity by container (zambil) not permissible in salam?
A) Because the container itself is a commodity that must be measured separately.
B) Because the quantity of the commodity may vary due to compression or expansion, leading to uncertainty.
C) Because the container is not a standard unit recognized by Islamic law.
D) Because the container might be made of impure material.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, salam requires precise quantity; a container is not a valid measure because of potential variation from compression.

Minimum Period for Salam – One Month or Sufficient Time?
Ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimum term for salam, explain the evidence from the hadith, and distinguish between the most sound opinion and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Salam requires a term; one month is the most sound minimum, but the operative fatwa is a term sufficient for acquisition.

In salam sales, the delivery term must be known. The Hanafi school debated the minimum length: is it fixed at one month, or flexible based on what is needed to obtain the goods? This lesson unpacks the evidence and the evolution of the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
What is the minimum term for salam according to the most sound Hanafi opinion?
The text says 'its minimum is one month according to the most sound opinion.'
2
Analyze the primary evidence
Examine the hadith citation 'to a known term' and how it is used to require a specific minimum period.
The author argues from the hadith and from the purpose of salam as a concession for the poor.
3
Understand the 'immediate' vs 'deferred' distinction
Why is less than one month considered immediate? The text uses the oath example from Hanafi fiqh.
If a debtor swears to pay immediately, paying within a month fulfills the oath; thus under a month is 'immediate'.
4
Contrast the two Hanafi positions
Muhammad al-Shaybani said one month; al-Karkhi said a term sufficient to acquire the goods. Note the arguments for and against each.
Al-Fath criticizes al-Karkhi for vagueness; al-Bahr al-Ra’iq supports him for practicality.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa prefers al-Karkhi's view. What is the practical implication for a salam contract today?
The operative ruling is a term that allows the seller to obtain the commodity, not necessarily one month.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأقله شهر في الأصح. ولنا: قوله في آخر الحديث: «إلى أجل معلوم»، وسوق الكلام لبيان شرط السلم لا لبيان الأجل، ولأن السلم إنما شرع رخصة للفقراء، فلا بد من مدة ليقدر على التحصيل والتتميم والإيصال والتسليم، لا يقال: إنه لو كان السلم مشروعاً لدفع حاجة الفقراء فينبغي أن يختص بحالة الإفلاس؛ لأن الإفلاس أمر باطن لا يمكن الوقوف على حقيقته. والشرع بنى هذه الرخصة على الحاجة، فبقي على السبب الظاهر الدال على الحاجة ليمكننا تعليق الحكم به، والبيع بالخسران دليل الحاجة، كذا في «الكفاية» وغيرها. [1] قوله: في الأصح؛ كذا روي عن محمد له، وعليه الفتوى؛ لأن ما دونه عاجل، والشهر وما فوقه آجل، بدليل مسألة اليمين، وهي أن المديون إذا حلف: ليقضين دينه عاجلاً فقضاه قبل تمام الشهر بر في يمينه، وإذا كان ما دون الشهر في حكم العاجل، كان الشهر في حكم العاجل. وقال الصدر الشهيد: والصحيح ما رواه الكرخي له أنه مقدر بما يمكن فيه تحصيل المسلم فيه. وقال في «الفتح»: وهو جدير بأن لا يصح؛ لأنه لا ضابط يتحقق فيه، وكذا ما عن الكَرْخِي من رواية أخرى: إنه ينظر إلى مقدار المسلم فيه، وإلى عرف الناس في تأجيل مثله، وكل هذا تنفتح فيه المنازعات بخلاف المقدار المعين من الزمان. انتهى. وقال في «البحر الرائق»: هو جدير بأن يصح ويعول عليه فقط؛ لأن من الأشياء ما لا يمكن تحصيله في شهر فيؤدّي التقدير به إلى عدم حصول المقصود من الأجل، وهو القدرة على تحصيله. انتهى. وعليه الفتوى.
And its minimum is one month according to the most sound opinion. Our evidence: His statement at the end of the Hadith: 'for a known term', and the context of the speech is to explain the condition of salam, not to explain the term. And because salam was legislated as a concession for the poor, so a period is necessary so that he can acquire, complete, deliver, and hand over. It cannot be said: if salam were legislated to meet the need of the poor, then it should be restricted to the state of insolvency because insolvency is an internal matter that cannot be verified. The Lawgiver built this concession on need, so it remains attached to the apparent cause indicating need so that we can attach the ruling to it; selling at a loss is evidence of need. Similarly in al-Kifayah and others. [1] His statement: 'according to the most sound' – thus it is narrated from Muhammad, and the fatwa is upon it, because what is less than it is immediate, and a month and above is deferred, as evidenced by the issue of oath: if a debtor swears that he will pay his debt immediately, and he pays it before the completion of a month, his oath is valid. If what is less than a month is considered immediate, then a month is considered deferred. And al-Sadr al-Shahid said: The correct view is what al-Karkhi narrated from him that it is estimated by what is possible to obtain the salam commodity. And it is said in al-Fath: It is deserving that it be invalid because there is no verified standard. Similarly, another narration from al-Karkhi: look at the quantity of the salam commodity and the custom of people in deferring similar items; all of these open the door to disputes, unlike the fixed amount of time. End. And it is said in al-Bahr al-Ra’iq: It is deserving that it be correct and relied upon only because some things cannot be obtained in a month, so the estimation leads to failure to achieve the goal of the term, which is the ability to obtain it. End. And the fatwa is upon it.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-madhhab dispute. It states that the minimum period for a salam contract is one month according to the most sound opinion (al-ashah). The proof is from the Hadith 'to a known term' (ilā ajal ma‘lūm), which indicates that a term is required. The author argues that the purpose of salam is a concession for the poor to obtain capital; they need time to produce and deliver the goods. The Shafi‘i objection that it should be restricted to insolvency is refuted because insolvency is internal and cannot be verified; instead, the apparent cause is need, indicated by selling at a loss. Then the text discusses the disagreement: Muhammad (al-Shaybani) reportedly set the minimum at one month, and fatwa is on this because less than a month is considered immediate (like in oath cases). However, al-Sadr al-Shahid reports from al-Karkhi that the term should be enough to obtain the commodity. Al-Fath criticizes this as having no fixed standard, leading to disputes. Al-Bahr al-Ra’iq supports al-Karkhi's view as more suitable because some goods cannot be produced in a month. The final fatwa is according to al-Karkhi's view.

Key Terms
الأجل (al-ajal): The fixed term for delivery in a salam contract; must be known and at least one month (or sufficient to acquire goods).
العاجل (al-‘ājil): Immediate delivery; less than a month is considered immediate in Hanafi law, as deduced from oath rulings.
الآجل (al-ājil): Deferred delivery; one month or more is considered deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One week
B) One month
C) Three months
D) There is no minimum; any known term suffices

Key Takeaway
The most sound Hanafi opinion sets the minimum salam term at one month, but the operative fatwa is a term sufficient for acquiring the commodity.

Minimum Term in Salam: The One-Month Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on minimum term for salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum term for a salam contract in the Hanafi school, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and justify the mufta bihi view of one month.

Core Idea
The minimum term for salam is one month — clear, dispute-proof, and supported by the hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a key condition of the salam contract: the minimum term. While the hadith requires a 'known term', Hanafi scholars debated the minimum duration. The author presents the relied-upon view of one month, the evidence, and the reasoning behind it, while also noting other opinions within the school. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate ikhtilaf and apply tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mufta bihi ruling
The passage states that the minimum term is one month according to the most correct view (al-asahh). This is the position relied upon for fatwa, as narrated from Imam Muhammad.
This is the operative ruling; all fatwa should be based on this.
2
Analyze the dalil
The key evidence is the hadith phrase 'to a known term' (إلى أجل معلوم). The author also provides the rationale: salam is a concession for the poor seller, who needs sufficient time to acquire and deliver the goods.
The dalil is zanni in its indication of the specific minimum, but binding in requiring a known term.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars argued for three days (by analogy with khiyar al-shart) or more than half a day (based on immediate vs. deferred). Al-Karkhi suggested the term should be sufficient to procure the goods.
These views are weaker (marjuh) and not relied upon for fatwa.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The one-month view prevails because it avoids disputes (gharar) and provides a clear, verifiable standard. The oath example shows that less than a month is considered immediate, reinforcing that a month is the minimum deferment.
Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supported al-Karkhi's view but the majority maintains one month.
5
Confirm authority weight
The one-month view is from Zahir al-Riwayah (narrated from Muhammad) and is upheld in Fath al-Qadir and Radd al-Muhtar. This gives it the highest authority in the school.
Always check the authority weight when citing a view: this is mufta bihi.

And its minimum is one month according to the most correct view. Our evidence: His statement at the end of the hadith: 'to a known term.' And the context of the speech is for explaining the condition of salam, not for explaining the term. And because salam was legislated as a concession for the poor, so a period is necessary so that they can be able to acquire, complete, deliver, and hand over. [1] His statement: 'according to the most correct view'; thus it is narrated from Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa; because what is less than it is immediate, and a month and above is deferred, evidenced by the issue of the oath: that if a debtor swears: 'I will surely repay my debt immediately' and he repays it before the completion of the month, he is righteous in his oath. And if what is less than a month is in the ruling of immediate, then a month is in the ruling of immediate. And al-Sadr al-Shahid said: the correct view is what al-Karkhi narrated from him, that it is estimated by what is possible therein to acquire the subject matter. And in al-Fath it said: it is worthy of being unsound, because there is no verifiable standard in it. Similarly, from al-Karkhi another narration: that one looks at the quantity of the subject matter and the custom of the people in deferring its like. All of this opens the door to disputes, unlike the specified quantity of time. End quote. And in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq it said: it is worthy of being sound and relied upon exclusively, because among things there are those that cannot be acquired in a month, so the estimation by it leads to failure to achieve the purpose of the term, which is the ability to acquire. End quote. And upon it is the fatwa, as in Fath al-Qadir and Radd al-Muhtar. And he only said 'according to the most correct view' because it has been said: its minimum is three days, and it has been said: more than half a day.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the minimum term in a salam contract. The relied-upon view (al-asahh) is that the minimum is one month, and this is what Imam Muhammad narrated and what is chosen for fatwa. The evidence from hadith is the phrase 'to a known term' in the salam hadith, which indicates the necessity of defining a term. The rationale is that salam is a concession for the needy seller, so a sufficient period must be given to allow him to acquire and deliver the goods. The author then addresses a potential objection: if the purpose is need, it should be limited to insolvent persons. But the Law takes the apparent cause (the sale itself indicating need) rather than hidden insolvency. The author also discusses differing views within the school: some (Ahmad ibn Abi Umran) said it is three days by analogy with the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), but this is rejected because three days is the maximum for option, not the minimum for term. Others (Abu Bakr al-Razi) said it is more than half a day, considering any payment after the session as deferred. The author then mentions al-Karkhi's view that the term should be sufficient for acquiring the goods, and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supports this as sound, but the dominant position remains one month to avoid disputes. The fatwa is on one month, as stated in Fath al-Qadir and Radd al-Muhtar.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The fixed future date for delivery of the subject matter in a salam contract.
سلّم (salam): A forward sale where price is paid immediately for goods to be delivered later.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most correct view within the school, used to indicate the mufta bihi position.
كفاية (kifayah): Reference to the Hanafi work 'Al-Kifayah' by Imam al-Halabi, a commentary on Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what is the minimum term in a salam contract?
A) Three days
B) More than half a day
C) One month
D) Sufficient time to acquire goods

Key Takeaway
The minimum term for salam is one month, as per the mufta bihi Hanafi view, to ensure a clear deferment and avoid disputes.

Specification of Capital Quantity in Salam: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on capital quantity specification with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on specifying capital quantity in salam and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding physical identification.

Core Idea
Physical identification of capital does not waive quantity specification according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn disagree.

In a salam contract, the capital (ra's al-mal) may be goods sold by volume, weight, or count. The contract is inherently tied to quantity, so its specification seems necessary. However, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises when the capital is physically identified at the time of contract: does pointing remove the need to state the quantity? This lesson examines the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, and the practical implications for valid salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the dispute is about the necessity of specifying the quantity of capital in salam when the capital is physically identified (mu'ayyan). The source text frames this as a difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
The capital here is of the type sold by volume, weight, or count, not by measurement (dhira').
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, even if the capital is physically pointed to and identified, its quantity must still be specified. The contract is tied to the quantity (mira'at al-miqdar), so ambiguity remains without a stated measure.
This is the default position and the one emphasised in the text.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if the capital is physically identified (mu'ayyan bil-ishara), no further quantity specification is needed. Physical identification itself removes uncertainty.
This view is also transmitted and considered legitimate.
4
Note the exception for dhira'i goods
When the capital is sold by linear measurement (dhira') and is pointed to, all Hanafi authorities agree that quantity specification is not required. This consensus (ittifaq) is mentioned to clarify the scope of the dispute.
This shows that the dispute is limited to kayli, wazni, and `adadi commodities.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, a salam contract with physically identified capital is invalid if quantity is not stated. Under the Sahibayn, such a contract is valid. The mufta bihi is typically Abu Hanifa's view for precaution, though the text does not explicitly tarjih.
This affects validity rulings in everyday salam transactions.

And the quantity of the capital in the case of (goods sold by) volume, weight, and count: because the contract in these is tied to the quantity, so it is necessary to state its quantity. This is [the view] of Abu Hanifa. But according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if the capital is physically identified, then it is not needed to state its quantity. [2] His statement: 'and it has been said...' etc: this was said by Abu Bakr al-Razi and some of Zufar's companions; because the immediate is that which is received in the session, and the deferred is that which is delayed from it, and the session usually does not remain more than half a day. [4] His statement: 'physically identified'; it is an indication of the point of difference between the Imam and the two companions. Commentary
This passage addresses the requirement to specify the quantity of the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract when the capital consists of goods sold by volume, weight, or count (kayli, wazni, `adadi). The rule is that since the contract is based on quantity, the quantity must be stated. However, there is a well-known intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, even if the capital is physically identified and pointed to at the contract, its quantity must still be specified. According to the Sahibayn, if the capital is physically identified (mu'ayyanan bil-ishara), then there is no need to state its quantity because pointing removes ambiguity. The text then notes that goods sold by measurement (dhira'i) are an exception: if the capital is of that type and is pointed to, all agree that specification of quantity is not required. The passage also includes an unrelated clarification about the minimum term discussion previously, emphasizing that the estimation of one month is for the minimum term, not the maximum.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital (price) paid in salam, which can be in cash or goods.
كيلي (kayli): Goods sold by volume (e.g., grains).
وزني (wazni): Goods sold by weight (e.g., metals).
عددي (`adadi): Goods sold by count (e.g., eggs, but not homogenous items).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The contract is invalid because the quantity of a kayli good must be specified even if physically identified
C) The contract is valid only if the grain is sold by volume, not weight
D) The contract is invalid only if the bag is not sealed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, for salam with capital goods sold by volume, weight, or count, physical identification does not waive the need to specify quantity according to Imam Abu Hanifa, though his two companions disagreed.

Specifying the Amount of Salam Principal: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Ruling on necessity of stating Salam principal amount with Hanafi internal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the amount of the Salam principal must be stated when the principal is a specific item, and evaluate the proofs of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires stating the exact amount of a kayli/wazni/adadi principal even if present; the Sahibayn accept pointing as sufficient.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah presents a key internal Hanafi dispute regarding the Salam contract. When the principal (ra's al-mal) is a specific item like a pile of wheat, do the parties need to state its exact quantity? The answer differs between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, with each side offering a rigorous legal proof. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's scope
The matn states that amount must be stated in kayli, wazni, adadi principal. The sharh reveals this is Abu Hanifa's view, while the Sahibayn hold that if the principal is mu'ayyan (specific and pointed to), no amount statement is needed. Confirm which item types are excluded (madhru' – by agreement no amount needed).
The exception for madhru' (length-measured) items is unanimous.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's proof (qiyas)
The Sahibayn argue that pointing (isharah) achieves identification just as in the price of a sale or rental of hire. In those contracts, if you say 'I bought with these dirhams' without stating the number, it is valid because the dirhams are specific. The same qiyas applies to Salam principal.
They compare to thaman (price) and ujrah (rent).
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's proof (istihsan-based reasoning)
Abu Hanifa argues that part of the principal might be counterfeit (zuyuf) and not replaceable in the session. If the amount is unknown, the parties cannot determine how much remains or what must be returned in case of defect, leading to dispute or riba. This risk exists even if the item is pointed to.
He uses a hypothetical scenario to show necessity of precise quantity.
4
Note the distinction between kayli/wazni/adadi and madhru'
For madhru' items (like cloth), the contract does not depend on the amount; pointing suffices by agreement. Abu Hanifa's objection does not apply because length is an attribute, not the essence of the substitute.
This distinction is crucial in fiqh reasoning.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Two examples: (1) 'I advance these dirhams to you in a kurr of wheat' without stating dirham weight; (2) 'I advance this wheat to you in a mann of saffron' without stating wheat amount. According to Abu Hanifa, the contract is invalid (fasid); according to the Sahibayn, it is valid (sahih).
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view.

And for Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that sometimes part of the capital is counterfeit, and it cannot be replaced in the session. So if he does not know its amount, he does not know how much remains, and perhaps he cannot obtain the object of salam, so he needs to return the capital. So it must be known, contrary to the case where the capital is a specific cloth, for the contract is not tied to its amount, so stating the amount of the capital is not required. Then he branched from this issue two issues, saying: According to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to them, even if he says: 'I have advanced these dirhams to you in a kurr of wheat' and does not state the weight of the dirhams, or says: 'I have advanced this wheat to you in a mann of saffron' and does not state the amount of the wheat, it is not valid according to him, but it is valid according to them. So if the measured or weighed item is made the price of the sold item or the rental, as if he says: 'I bought with these dirhams' or says: 'I hired with these dirhams' and does not state their amount, it is permissible. So likewise here, and it becomes like a cloth if it is made the capital, for it is permissible even if he does not state its length. [2] His saying: 'And for Abu Hanifa...etc.'; its summary: The one receiving the advance may be unable to deliver the object of salam after spending the capital, so he does not know how much to return, and that leads to dispute or to riba. And if the one receiving the advance spends something from the capital then finds the remaining defective and returns it and does not replace it in the session of return, then the contract is void to the extent of what he returned. So if the capital is not known, he does not know how much of the salam has been undone nor how much remains. And if it is known, from the amount returned, the amount that has decreased from the salam is known, so he knows the amount remaining. And anything that leads to ignorance of the object of salam must be avoided even if it is hypothetical. Do you not see the consideration of hypothetical destruction in the dates of a specific wall and in a specific measure and weight? Contrary to the case where the capital is measured by length, because length is an attribute in the measured-by-length item, and substitutes do not correspond to attributes. (3) His saying: 'so he needs to return the capital'; and if it is unknown in amount, that return is impossible. [4] His saying: 'for the contract...etc.'; its summary: length is an attribute in the cloth, do you not see that when...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the necessity of stating the amount (قدر) of the principal (رأس المال) in a Salam contract. The matn states that the amount must be stated when the principal is measured by volume (كيلي), weight (وزني), or count (عددي). The sharh clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if the principal is a specific, identifiable item (معين), such as a particular pile of wheat pointed to at the time of the contract, then stating its amount is not required because the purpose of identification is achieved by pointing (إشارة). The commentary then discusses two earlier opinions about the minimum term (أجل) of Salam (three days or more than half a day), but the main focus is the principal. The Sahibayn's proof is that pointing suffices, just as in the case of the price in a sale or the rental in a hire contract: if one says 'I bought with these dirhams' without stating the number, it is valid because the dirhams are specific. Abu Hanifa's proof is that part of the capital might be counterfeit (زُيُوف) and cannot be replaced in the session; if the amount is unknown, the parties will not know how much remains to be returned or how much of the Salam is undone, leading to dispute or riba. The text also distinguishes between measured-by-volume/weight/count items (which require amount specification even if pointed to, according to Abu Hanifa) and measured-by-length items (مذروع) like cloth, where pointing suffices by agreement because length is an attribute, not the essence of the substitute. Two illustrative examples are given: if someone says 'I advance these dirhams to you in a kurr of wheat' without stating the dirham weight, or 'I advance this wheat to you in a mann of saffron' without stating the wheat amount, the contract is invalid according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to the Sahibayn. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa, as the text presents his reasoning as stronger and the Sahibayn's view is contrasted.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The principal or capital advanced in a Salam contract, which is paid in advance by the buyer (muslim) to the seller (muslam ilayh).
السلم (al-salam): A sale where payment is made in advance for goods to be delivered later at a specified time, with the price (principal) being paid immediately.
معين (mu'ayyan): Specific, identifiable by pointing or reference, as opposed to a generic or undefined item.
إشارة (isharah): Pointing to a specific item as a means of identification.
كيلي (kayli): Items measured by volume, such as grains and dates.
وزني (wazni): Items measured by weight, such as gold, silver, and certain foodstuffs.
عددي (adadi): Items counted by number, such as eggs, nuts, or coins when not used as currency.
مذروع (madhru'): Items measured by length, such as cloth or land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a Salam contract where the principal is a specific pile of wheat (kayli) but its weight is not stated?
A) The contract is valid because the wheat is present and pointed to.
B) The contract is invalid because the amount of wheat is unknown.
C) The contract is valid only if the wheat is measured by volume.
D) The contract is suspended until the amount is stated.

Key Takeaway
In Salam contracts, Abu Hanifa requires the exact amount of kayli, wazni, and adadi principals to be stated, even if the item is present and pointed to, to prevent future uncertainty and riba.

Specifying Capital Quantity in Salam: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on capital condition in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on capital specification in salam and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
In salam, Abu Hanifa demands explicit quantity; Sahibayn accept pointing—risk of future dispute dictates the stricter view.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the conditions of capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract. While the Sahibayn analogize to price and wage, Abu Hanifa warns of potential disputes and riba if the quantity remains unknown. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to evaluate conditions in deferred delivery sales.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the core ruling: Abu Hanifa requires explicit statement of weight/measure; Sahibayn accept pointing to the physical capital.
Both are Hanafi imams, so this is an internal school ikhtilaf.
2
Unpack the Sahibayn's proof
They analogize to price in sale and wage in hire, where pointing suffices, and to cloth as capital where length need not be stated.
Qiyas is used; note the types of analogy.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's counter-proof
He argues that without known quantity, returning defective part (zuyuf) leads to uncertainty about remaining capital, potentially causing riba or dispute.
He distinguishes from cloth because contract is attached to quantity.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: a salam contract where capital is pointed to but not weighed is invalid per Abu Hanifa, valid per Sahibayn.
This affects fatwa in practice.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary favors Abu Hanifa's view; thus the operative ruling requires explicit specification of capital quantity.
Weight of later Hanafi masters supports this.

لأن المقصود يحصل بالإشارة كما في الثَّمن والأجرة. ولأبي حنيفة ) الله أنَّه ربَّما يكون بعض رأس المالِ زُيُوفاً، ولا يُستَبْدَلُ في المجلس، فلو لم يعلم قَدْرُهُ لا يَدْرِي كم بَقِيَ ، وربما لا يَقْدِرُ على تحصيلِ الْمُسَلَّمِ فيه، فيحتاجُ إلى رد رأس المال، فيجب أن يكون معلوماً بخلاف ما إذا كان رأس المالِ ثوباً معيناً فإن العقد لا يتعلق بمقداره ، فلا يَحِبُ بيانُ قَدْرِ رأس المال ، ثمّ فرع على هذه المسألة مسألتين ، فقال : فعند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما، حتى لو قال : أسلمتُ إليك هذه الدراهم في كُرِّبُرٌ ولم يُبيِّن وزن الدراهم، أو قال : أسلمتُ إليك هذا البُرَّ في مَنْ زعفران، ولم يُبيِّن قدرَ البُرِّ لا يصح . عنده ، ويصح عندهما. [ ١ ] قوله : لأن...الخ ؛ حاصله أن المقصود هو الإعلام لتحصيل القدرة على التسليم، وانقطاع النزاع، وذا يحصل بالإشارة ؛ لأنها أبلغ أسباب التعريف ، فلا يشترط معها بيان القدرة، كما لا يشترط في الثمن في البيع، والأجرة في الإجارة. فإذا جُعِل المكيل أو الموزون ثمن المبيع أو الأجرة كأن يقول : اشتريت بهذه الدراهم، أو يقول : استأجرتُ بهذه الدراهم، ولم يُبين مقدارهما يجوز، فكذا هاهنا ، وصار كالثوب إذا جعلَ رأس المال فإنّه يجوز ، وإن لم يُبين ذرعانه. [٢] قوله : ولأبي حنيفة ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ المسلَّمَ إليه قد يعجز عن تسليم المسلّم فيه بعدما أنفق رأس المال، فلا يدري كم يردّ ، وذلك يفضي إلى المنازعة، أو إلى الربا، وإنَّ المسلم إليه إذا أنفق شيئاً من رأس المالِ ثم وجد الباقي معيباً فـرده ولم يستبدله في مجلس الردّ ، فإنَّ العقد يبطل بعقد ما ردَّه ، فإذا لم يكن رأس المال معلوماً لم يدر ما انتقض من السلّم ولا ما بقي منه ، وإذا كان معلوماً من قدر المردود قدر ما انتقص من السلم، فيعلم قدر ما بقي منه. وما يفضي إلى جهالة المسلّم فيه يجب الاحتراز عنه وإن كان موهوماً، ألا ترى إلى اعتبار الهلاكِ الموهوم في تمر حائط بعينه، وفي مكيال وميزان بعينه، بخلاف ما لو كان رأس المال مذروعاً ؛ لأنَّ الذرع وصف في المذروع، والأبدالُ لا تقابل الأوصاف. (۳) قوله : فيحتاج إلى ردّ رأس المال ؛ وإذا كان مجهول القدر تعدر ذلك الرد. [٤]قوله : فإن العقد...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ الذرع وصف في الثوب، ألا ترى أنه إذا وجدَهُ زائداً على المسمَّى سَلّم له الزيادة، وإذا وجده ناقصاً لم يحط شيئاً من الثمن كما تقدم تحقيقه، فلا يتعلق العقد بمقدار الثوب، وكلامنا ليس في ذلك، بل فيما يتعلق العقد بمقداره، فكان قياساً مع الفارق.
Because the purpose is achieved by pointing, just as in the case of the price and the wage. And Abu Hanifa argued that sometimes part of the capital may be defective coins and not exchanged in the session, so if its quantity is unknown, one does not know how much remains, and perhaps he cannot obtain the salam commodity, so he needs to return the capital. Therefore, it must be known, unlike the case when the capital is a specific cloth, for the contract is not attached to its length, so stating the length is not required. Then he branched from this issue two issues, saying: According to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions, even if he said: 'I have given salam to you these dirhams for a karr of wheat' and did not specify the weight of the dirhams, or said: 'I have given salam to you this wheat for a mann of saffron' and did not specify the quantity of the wheat, it is invalid according to him, but valid according to them. [1] His saying: 'Because...etc.': Its summary is that the purpose is informing to achieve ability to deliver and cessation of dispute, and this is achieved by pointing because it is the most effective means of identification. So specification of quantity is not required along with it, just as it is not required regarding the price in sale and the wage in hire. If a measurable or weighable item is made the price of the sold item or the wage, such as saying 'I bought with these dirhams' or 'I hired with these dirhams' without specifying their quantity, it is valid, so here too. And it becomes like cloth when made capital, it is valid even if its length is not specified. [2] His saying: 'And for Abu Hanifa...etc.': Its summary is that the recipient of the capital may become unable to deliver the salam commodity after spending the capital, and he does not know how much to return, and that leads to dispute or riba. And if the recipient spends some of the capital, then finds the rest defective and returns it without exchanging it in the session of return, then the contract is invalidated to the extent returned. So if the capital is unknown, he does not know how much of the salam is annulled and how much remains. When it is known, from the quantity returned he knows how much of the salam is diminished, and he knows how much remains. And anything that leads to ignorance of the salam commodity must be avoided even if potential. Do you not see the consideration of potential destruction in the dates of a specific wall, and in a specific measure and weight? Unlike the case when the capital is measured by length, because length is a description in the measured item, and substitutes do not counter descriptions. (3) His saying: 'so he needs to return the capital'; when it is unknown in quantity, that return becomes impossible. [4] His saying: 'Then the contract...etc.': Its summary is that length is a description in the cloth. Do you not see that if he finds it exceeding the named length, the excess is given to him as a gift, and if he finds it less, nothing is deducted from the price as has been previously verified. So the contract is not attached to the quantity of the cloth, and our discussion is not about that, but rather about what the contract is attached to its quantity, so it is an analogy with a difference.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the condition of knowing the quantity of capital in a salam contract. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that pointing to the capital (e.g., 'these dirhams') suffices for specification, analogizing to the price in sale and wage in hire, where pointing works. They further analogize to when the capital is a cloth: even if its length is not mentioned, the contract is valid because the contract is not tied to the cloth's length. Abu Hanifa responds that without knowing the capital's exact quantity, future problems may arise: if part of the capital is defective and returned, one cannot determine how much remains, leading to dispute or even riba (usury). He also argues that the analogy to cloth fails because the contract is not attached to the cloth's length, whereas in salam the contract is attached to the capital's quantity. The text then elaborates on the hypothetical scenarios, emphasizing the need to avoid potential ignorance.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid upfront in a salam contract; its quantity must be known according to Abu Hanifa.
الإشارة (al-ishara): Pointing to the specific physical capital without stating its quantity; considered sufficient by Sahibayn.
زُيُوف (zuyuf): Defective coins that are not accepted at face value; their presence in capital can cause uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the capital must be of a specific type
B) Because the seller may need to return part of the capital if defective, leading to dispute or riba
C) Because the buyer must know the exact weight of the capital for zakat calculation
D) Because the capital cannot be coins

Key Takeaway
In salam, Abu Hanifa's view that capital quantity must be explicitly known is the mufta bihi position, emphasizing precaution against future uncertainty and riba.

Salam with Multiple Types or Currencies: The Condition of Capital Allocation
Rules for salam when capital or commodity involves two types or currencies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam involving multiple types of goods or currencies and apply the condition that the capital amount for each part must be explicitly known.

Core Idea
Salam requires certainty; if capital allocation is conjectural, the contract is invalid.

This passage applies a key condition of salam—that the capital amount must be known—to two specific scenarios: when the commodity consists of two types (e.g., wheat and barley) or when the capital is paid in two currencies (dirhams and dinars). The text walks through Abu Hanifa's reasoning for invalidating such contracts, contrasting with the Sahibayn’s position that pointing to the items suffices. Understanding this example will clarify how rigorous the Hanafi school is in avoiding uncertainty (gharar) in salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed condition
The act is a salam contract where the capital or commodity involves multiple components. The disputed condition is whether the specific capital allocated to each component must be stated explicitly.
2
Analyze the first scenario: two types of commodity
A buyer pays 100 dirhams for a karr of wheat and a karr of barley, without specifying how much of the 100 is for each. Abu Hanifa rules invalid because the capital would be divided by estimated value, which is conjectural. The Sahibayn accept because the specific items are pointed to.
If the commodity were one type, the capital divides equally, making it valid.
3
Analyze the second scenario: two currencies as capital
A buyer pays 10 dirhams and 10 dinars for 10 qafiz of wheat, without specifying the weight of each currency. Abu Hanifa rules invalid because without knowing the weight, the share of each from the wheat is unknown. The Sahibayn again accept pointing.
4
Identify the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa’s position rests on the general requirement that the capital amount must be known (miqdar ra's al-mal ma'lum). When multiple components are involved, ignorance of the allocation introduces uncertainty.
The Sahibayn argue that physical designation (ishara) removes uncertainty.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In both scenarios, the contract is fasid (invalid) per Abu Hanifa, but valid per the Sahibayn. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) follows Abu Hanifa, making such contracts invalid unless the capital portions are explicitly stated.
This highlights the importance of drafting salam contracts with precise allocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يَجُزِ السَّلَمُ في جنسين بلا بيانِ رأس مالِ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ولا بنقدينِ بلا بيان حصة كل منهما من المسلم فيه. [1] قوله : في جنسين بلا بيان...الخ ؛ صورتهُ : أنه أسلم مئة درهم في كُرِّبُرٌ وكُرّ شعير، ولم يبين رأس مال كلِّ واحدٍ منها ، فلا يجوز عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ إعلام قدرِ رأس المال شرط. فينقسم ا ـمُ المئة على البر والشعير باعتبار القيمة، وهي تعرف بالظنّ، فلا يكونُ مقدار رأس مال كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، حتى لو كان من جنس واحد يصح؛ لأنَّ رأس المالِ منقسم عليهما على السواء، وعندهما يجوز؛ لأنَّ الإشارة إلى العين تكفي لجواز العقد، وقد وجدت. [٢]قوله : ولا بنقدين؛ صورته أنه أسلم عشرة دراهم وعشرة دنانير في عشرة قفيز بر، لم يجز عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ الدراهم والدنانير المذكورة إذا لم تعلم وزناً يلزم عدم بيانِ حصة كلّ واحدٍ منهما، من المسلم فيه، وعندهما يجوز؛ لأنَّ الإشارة وجدت، وهي كافية لجواز العقد.
So salam is not valid in two types without stating the capital of each one, nor in two currencies without stating the share of each from the salam commodity. [1] His saying: 'in two types without stating...etc.': Its form is that he paid salam of one hundred dirhams for a karr of wheat and a karr of barley, and did not specify the capital of each, so it is invalid according to the Imam because informing of the quantity of capital is a condition. So the hundred is divided between the wheat and barley by estimation of value, which is known by conjecture, so the capital amount of each is not known. Even if it were of one type, it would be valid because the capital is divided equally between them. And according to them (the two companions), it is valid because pointing to the specific object suffices for the validity of the contract, and it has occurred. [2] His saying: 'and not in two currencies'; its form is that he paid salam of ten dirhams and ten dinars for ten qafiz of wheat; it is invalid according to the Imam because the mentioned dirhams and dinars, if their weight is not known, it necessitates not stating the share of each from the salam commodity. According to them, it is valid because pointing has occurred, and it suffices for validity of the contract.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents two branch rulings from the previous dispute, both showing Abu Hanifa's requirement for explicit designation of capital portions. The first scenario: a salam contract where the capital is 100 dirhams and the commodity includes two types (wheat and barley). Abu Hanifa requires specifying how much of the capital is for each type; otherwise, allotment would be by estimated value (conjecture), which is uncertain. If the commodity is one type, the capital is divided equally, so it is valid. The Sahibayn accept the contract because pointing to the specific items suffices. The second scenario: capital consists of two currencies (dirhams and dinars) for a single commodity (wheat). Again, Abu Hanifa requires knowing the weight of each currency to determine the share from the commodity; Sahibayn accept pointing. In both cases, the practical effect is that those contracts are invalid per Abu Hanifa but valid per Sahibayn.

Key Terms
جنسين (jinsayn): Two different types of goods (e.g., wheat and barley) as the subject matter of salam.
نقدين (naqdyn): Two different currencies (dirhams and dinars) as the capital of salam.
حصة (hissa): The portion of the salam commodity that corresponds to each part of the capital.

Key Takeaway
In salam, when multiple goods or currencies are involved, Abu Hanifa requires explicit allocation of the capital to each component; otherwise the contract is fasid due to uncertainty.

Place of delivery in salam when transportation is costly
Stipulating delivery place for salam commodity requiring transport cost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition regarding place of delivery in a salam contract when transport costs are significant, and apply it to analogous contracts (price, wage, division).

Core Idea
A salam contract's validity requires specifying the delivery place if transporting the commodity incurs expense.

When you order goods with advance payment (salam), you must agree on where and when delivery occurs. But if moving those goods would cost a lot, that place becomes a critical detail. The Hanafi school teaches that failing to name it can make the contract defective—just like leaving the price or wage uncertain in other transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is the salam contract; the condition is that the place of delivery must be specified when transport cost is significant (mu'na).
2
Recognize the trigger
The trigger is 'in kana hamluhu mu'na' — if its transport involves expense. This means the cost of moving the commodity is material, not trivial.
3
Apply the analogy
The text states 'wa mithluhu al-thaman, wa al-ujra, wa al-qismah' — the same rule applies to price in sale, wage in hire, and division of property. In each, uncertainty about a costly location invalidates.
4
Understand the consequence of omission
If the place is not specified when transport is costly, the contract is fasid (defective) due to jahalah (uncertainty) in the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومكان إيفاء مسلّم فيه إن كان الحمله مؤنةً، ومثله الثّمن، والأجرة، والقسمة. [٣] قوله : لحمله مؤنة ؛ قال في «المغرب» (۱) : الحمل: بالفتح، مصدرُ حَمَلَ
And the place of delivery of the salam commodity, if its transport involves expense, and similar is the price, the wage, and division. [3] His saying: 'because its transport involves expense'; he said in al-Maghrib: 'al-haml' with fat-ha is the infinitive of 'hamala'.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage states a rule: the place where the salam commodity is to be delivered must be specified if transporting it incurs significant cost. This is analogous to the price in a sale, the wage in a hire contract, and the division of property. The commentary briefly notes the definition of 'haml' (transport) from the lexicon al-Maghrib, indicating that it is the verbal noun meaning carrying. The implication is that failure to specify a place with transport cost would lead to uncertainty (jahalah) in the obligation, thus the contract may be invalid or the specification becomes necessary.

Key Terms
مكان الإيفاء (makan al-ifa'): The place where delivery of the salam commodity is to take place.
مؤنة الحمولة (mu'nat al-hamula): The expense of transportation; if significant, the place of delivery must be agreed upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if the commodity is perishable

Key Takeaway
The delivery place in a salam contract must be specified when transport costs are material; otherwise, the contract is invalid due to uncertainty.

Capital Allocation in Salam for Two Commodities or Currencies
Explanatory commentary on matn of al-Wiqaya regarding capital allocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the validity of a salam contract when capital is paid for two different commodities or currencies without specifying the share of each, and explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Salam with mixed capital is invalid per Abu Hanifa unless each share is known; Sahibayn accept physical indication.

In salam contracts, the capital (ra's al-māl) must be known. But what if the buyer pays a lump sum for two different goods or two currencies? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major students on whether such a contract is valid. We will analyze their proofs and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer pays a fixed amount for two different commodities (e.g., wheat and barley) or two currencies (dirhams and dinars) without allocating the capital per item.
Same type (e.g., two grains of the same kind) is valid by default.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
The contract is invalid because the capital per item is unknown – dividing by value is conjectural (zann).
Knowledge of capital amount is a condition (shart) for validity.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The contract is valid because physical indication (ishāra ilā al-'ayn) suffices; pointing to the goods removes uncertainty.
They rely on custom and ease of transaction.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa emphasizes precise knowledge; Sahibayn accept indication as sufficient due to the nature of deferred contracts.
The divergence stems from different interpretations of the condition 'known capital'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, parties following Abu Hanifa must explicitly allocate capital shares; following Sahibayn, a general indication suffices.
Later Hanafi fatwas often favor Abu Hanifa for caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يَجُزِ السَّلَمُ في جنسين بلا بيانِ رأس مالِ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ولا بنقدينِ بلا بيانِ حصة كل منهما من المسلم فيه. ومكان إيفاء مسلّم فيه إن كان الحمله مؤنةً، ومثله الثّمن، والأجرة، والقسمة.

وجدَهُ زائداً على المسمَّى سَلّم له الزيادة، وإذا وجده ناقصاً لم يحط شيئاً من الثمن كما تقدم تحقيقه، فلا يتعلق العقد بمقدار الثوب، وكلامنا ليس في ذلك، بل فيما يتعلق العقد بمقداره، فكان قياساً مع الفارق.

قوله : في جنسين بلا بيان...الخ ؛ صورتهُ : أنه أسلم مئة درهم في كُرِّ بُرٍّ وكُرِّ شعير، ولم يبين رأس مال كلِّ واحدٍ منها ، فلا يجوز عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ إعلام قدرِ رأس المال شرط. فينقسم المئة على البر والشعير باعتبار القيمة، وهي تعرف بالظنّ، فلا يكونُ مقدار رأس مال كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، حتى لو كان من جنس واحد يصح؛ لأنَّ رأس المالِ منقسم عليهما على السواء، وعندهما يجوز؛ لأنَّ الإشارة إلى العين تكفي لجواز العقد، وقد وجدت.

قوله : ولا بنقدين؛ صورته أنه أسلم عشرة دراهم وعشرة دنانير في عشرة قفيز بر، لم يجز عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ الدراهم والدنانير المذكورة إذا لم تعلم وزناً يلزم عدم بيانِ حصة كلّ واحدٍ منهما من المسلم فيه، وعندهما يجوز؛ لأنَّ الإشارة وجدت، وهي كافية لجواز العقد.

قوله : لحمله مؤنة ؛ قال في «المغرب» : الحمل: بالفتح، مصدرُ حَمَلَ الشيء، ومنه ما له جمل ومؤنة، يعنون ما له ثقل يحتاج في حمله إلى ظهر أو أجرة حَمَّاله ، وبيانه في لفظ الأصل : ما له مؤنة. وفي «المنتخب»: باري بر گردن بر وارند. انتهى. ومؤنة بالفتح : باروكراني وهي فعولة، كذا في منتهى الأرب». قيل : ما له جمل ومؤنة : هو ما يكون بحال لو أمر إنساناً يحمله إلى مجلس القضاء لا يحمله مجاناً ، وقيل : هو ما يمكن رفعه بيد واحدة ، وقيل : ما يحتاج في نقله إلى المؤنة كالحنطة والشعير، وما لا يحتاجُ فيه إليها فهو مما لا مؤنة له، كالمسك والكافور، كذا في «الكفاية».

قوله وعندهما ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ التسليم موجب للعقد، فيتعين له موضع وجوده كما في البيع ؛ ولهذا وجب تسليم رأس مالِ السَّلَم في ذلك المكان، فكذا البدلُ الآخر، إذ العقـد يـوجب المساواة ؛ لأنَّ السبب الموجب للأحكام المتعلقة به، والتسليم من جملتها. فيتعين له موضعُ وجوده دلالة ما لم يعينا له مكاناً آخر بالنص، فيتعينُ له ذلك المكان ؛ لأنه يفوق الدلالة، وبه قالت الثلاثة ؛ كبيع وقرض وإتلاف وغصب، فإنَّ مكانها يتعيَّن لتسليم المبيع والقرض وبدل المتلف والمغصوب. ولأبي حنيفة : إنَّ تعيين مكان العقدِ إمَّا بالتعيين صريحاً أو ضرورة وجوب التسليم عليه في الحال ولم يوجد ، إذ السلم لا يجوز إلا مؤجلاً ، فيكون التسليم متأخراً ضرورة، بخلاف البيع والإتلاف والقرض والغصب ؛ لأنه واجب التسليم في الحال، فيتعين موضع وجود السبب ضرورة ؛ ولأنه لو تعيَّن مكان العقد لمـا جـاز تغييره بالشرط : كمكان البيع في حقِّ العين، وكتعين مكان العقد فيما إذا عقداً في لجة البحر، وفساده لا يخفى.
Salam is not permissible in two types without specifying the capital of each, nor in two currencies without specifying the share of each from the subject matter. And the place of delivery of the subject matter, if it has carrying expense, and likewise the price, the rent, and the division. He found it exceeding the named amount, he is given the excess, and if he finds it deficient, he does not reduce anything from the price as its verification preceded. So the contract is not attached to the amount of the cloth, and our speech is not about that, but rather about what the contract is attached to in its amount, so it is an analogy with a difference. He said: 'In two types without a statement... etc.' Its form: he paid one hundred dirhams for a kurr of wheat and a kurr of barley, and he did not specify the capital for each. So it is not permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa because informing the amount of capital is a condition. So the hundred is divided between wheat and barley based on value, which is known by conjecture, so the amount of capital for each is not known. Even if it is of one type, it is valid because the capital is divided equally between them. According to them (Sahibayn), it is permissible because indication to the tangible goods is sufficient for validity of the contract, and it has been found. He said: 'Nor in two currencies.' Its form: he paid ten dirhams and ten dinars for ten qafiz of wheat. It is not permissible according to Imam because if the mentioned dirhams and dinars are not known by weight, there is necessarily lack of statement of the share of each from the subject matter. According to them, it is permissible because indication has been found, and it is sufficient for validity of the contract. He said: 'If its carrying has expense.' He said in al-Mughrib: 'al-haml' with fatha is the source of 'hamala' the thing, and from it is something that has burden and expense, meaning something heavy that in carrying needs a back or the wage of a porter. Its explanation in the original wording: 'what has expense.' In al-Muntakhab: 'it is heavy on the neck.' End quote. And 'mūna' with fatha means heaviness, and it is a fu'ūlah pattern, as in Muntaha al-Arab. It is said: 'What has burden and expense' is that which if a person orders another to carry it to the court, he would not carry it for free. And it is said: It is what can be lifted with one hand. And it is said: It is what needs expense in its transport, like wheat and barley, and what does not need it is without expense, like musk and camphor, as in al-Kifaya. He said: 'According to them... etc.' Because delivery is necessitated by the contract, so its place of existence is determined as in sale. Hence delivery of the capital of salam was obligatory in that place, similarly the other substitute, since the contract requires equality; because the cause obligates the rulings pertaining to it, and delivery is among them. So its place of existence is determined by implication unless they specify another place by text, then that place is determined because it overrides implication. Thus said the three (Imam Shafi'i and Sahibayn?), like sale, loan, destruction, and usurpation, for their places are determined for delivery of the sold item, loan, substitute of destroyed and usurped. And for Abu Hanifa: the determination of the place of contract is either by explicit specification or necessity of obligatory immediate delivery, and it is not found, because salam is only valid with delay, so delivery is necessarily postponed, unlike sale, destruction, loan, and usurpation because delivery is obligatory immediately, so the place of existence of the cause is determined by necessity. And because if the place of contract were determined, it would not be permissible to change it by condition: like the place of sale regarding the tangible goods, and like determination of contract place if they contracted in the middle of the sea, and its invalidity is not hidden.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, discussing two important issues in salam (forward sale) contracts according to the Hanafi school. The first issue concerns salam where the capital (ra's al-mal) is paid in a lump sum for two different commodities (e.g., wheat and barley) or in two different currencies (dirhams and dinars). The text presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). According to Abu Hanifa, such a contract is invalid because the amount of capital allocated to each commodity or each currency is unknown – it would have to be determined by estimating market value, which is based on conjecture (zann). If the two commodities are of the same type (e.g., both wheat), the contract is valid because the capital is divided equally by default. The Sahibayn, however, consider the contract valid because the physical indication (ishāra ilā al-'ayn) of the capital is sufficient; the parties can simply point to a pile of dirhams and dinars, and that removes uncertainty. This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the role of precise quantification versus physical indication in contracts. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the later school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa for caution, but the Sahibayn's view is also recognized. The second issue is the place of delivery (makān īfā') of the subject matter (muslam fīhi). If the goods have a significant carrying cost (mūna) – defined as heavy items like wheat and barley that require hired labor to transport – then according to Abu Hanifa, the place of delivery must be explicitly specified in the contract. If not specified, the contract is invalid. The Sahibayn hold that the place of delivery is by default the place where the contract was made, analogizing to spot contracts like sale and loan, where immediate delivery is expected. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that salam is always deferred, so there is no urgency to fix the place contractually; the parties can later agree. He also argues that if the contract place were automatically the delivery place, the parties could not change it by condition, which is impractical. The text refutes the Sahibayn's analogy by pointing out that sale and loan involve immediate delivery, necessitating a fixed place; salam is different. The commentary also includes a brief digression distinguishing this case from a scenario where a buyer finds extra or less cloth – that is a different issue not related to quantity. Key definitions are provided for 'mūna' (expense) from various sources, emphasizing that the determining factor is whether the goods are heavy enough that a person would not carry them for free. Overall, this passage illustrates how Hanafi scholars balance textual requirements (shart) with practical considerations through qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-māl): The capital paid by the buyer in a salam contract; it must be known in amount and type.
مسلم فيه (muslam fīhi): The subject matter of the salam contract; the goods to be delivered at a future date.
إشارة إلى العين (ishāra ilā al-'ayn): Physical indication or pointing to the tangible goods/capital; considered sufficient by the Sahibayn to remove uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone pays 100 dirhams for a kurr of wheat and a kurr of barley in a salam contract without specifying how much of the capital goes to each, the contract is:
A) Valid because the capital is physically indicated.
B) Invalid because the capital share for each commodity is unknown and based on conjecture.
C) Valid if the two grains are of the same type.
D) Invalid only if the grains are of different types.

Key Takeaway
In salam, the capital must be precisely known per Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn accept physical indication, but the cautious practice follows Abu Hanifa for capital allocation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Place of Delivery in Salam for Goods with Carrying Expense
Commentary on matn of al-Wiqaya regarding delivery place and definition of mūna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the place of delivery in salam for goods with carrying expense, and apply the definitions of mūna to determine when specification is required.

Core Idea
For heavy goods, Abu Hanifa requires explicit delivery place; Sahibayn default to contract place.

In a salam contract, where should the seller deliver the goods? This lesson explores the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether the delivery place can be the contract location by default or must be explicitly stated. We will also define what constitutes 'carrying expense' (mūna) and how it affects the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Define mūna (carrying expense)
Learn the criteria: goods that require hired help or burden to transport, like wheat and barley, have mūna; light items like musk do not.
Multiple definitions exist: cannot be carried free to court, cannot be lifted with one hand, etc.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
For goods with mūna, the delivery place must be explicitly agreed; if not, the contract is invalid because deferred delivery creates uncertainty.
Salam is deferred, so there is no immediate necessity to fix the place.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The place of delivery is automatically the contract place, by analogy to spot transactions (sale, loan, etc.) where delivery is immediate.
They argue that the contract itself implies delivery at the contract location.
4
Analyze the qiyas debate
Sahibayn analogize salam to spot contracts; Abu Hanifa rejects the analogy because salam is deferred.
The key difference is the immediacy of delivery.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Following Abu Hanifa, parties must specify the delivery location for heavy goods; following Sahibayn, no specification is needed – the contract place suffices.
Later practice often adopts the Sahibayn's view for ease of transactions.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, discussing two important issues in salam (forward sale) contracts according to the Hanafi school. The first issue concerns salam where the capital (ra's al-mal) is paid in a lump sum for two different commodities (e.g., wheat and barley) or in two different currencies (dirhams and dinars). The text presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). According to Abu Hanifa, such a contract is invalid because the amount of capital allocated to each commodity or each currency is unknown – it would have to be determined by estimating market value, which is based on conjecture (zann). If the two commodities are of the same type (e.g., both wheat), the contract is valid because the capital is divided equally by default. The Sahibayn, however, consider the contract valid because the physical indication (ishāra ilā al-'ayn) of the capital is sufficient; the parties can simply point to a pile of dirhams and dinars, and that removes uncertainty. This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the role of precise quantification versus physical indication in contracts. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the later school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa for caution, but the Sahibayn's view is also recognized. The second issue is the place of delivery (makān īfā') of the subject matter (muslam fīhi). If the goods have a significant carrying cost (mūna) – defined as heavy items like wheat and barley that require hired labor to transport – then according to Abu Hanifa, the place of delivery must be explicitly specified in the contract. If not specified, the contract is invalid. The Sahibayn hold that the place of delivery is by default the place where the contract was made, analogizing to spot contracts like sale and loan, where immediate delivery is expected. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that salam is always deferred, so there is no urgency to fix the place contractually; the parties can later agree. He also argues that if the contract place were automatically the delivery place, the parties could not change it by condition, which is impractical. The text refutes the Sahibayn's analogy by pointing out that sale and loan involve immediate delivery, necessitating a fixed place; salam is different. The commentary also includes a brief digression distinguishing this case from a scenario where a buyer finds extra or less cloth – that is a different issue not related to quantity. Key definitions are provided for 'mūna' (expense) from various sources, emphasizing that the determining factor is whether the goods are heavy enough that a person would not carry them for free. Overall, this passage illustrates how Hanafi scholars balance textual requirements (shart) with practical considerations through qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms
مؤنة (mūna): Carrying expense or burden; goods that require hired help or are heavy enough that one would not carry them for free.
مسلم فيه (muslam fīhi): The subject matter of the salam contract; the goods to be delivered at a future date.
دلالة (dalāla): Implied meaning or indication; used here to refer to the logical implication that delivery place is the contract place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a salam contract is concluded for 100 kg of wheat (a heavy commodity) without specifying the delivery place, the contract is:
A) Valid, and delivery must occur at the contract place.
B) Invalid because wheat has mūna and the place must be specified.
C) Valid only if the seller agrees to deliver anywhere.
D) Invalid only if the wheat is more than 50 kg.

Key Takeaway
For goods with carrying expense, Abu Hanifa demands explicit delivery place; the Sahibayn accept the contract place by default, facilitating practice.
