Specifying Delivery Place in Salam for Heavy Goods: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delivery place in salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on specifying the delivery place in a salam contract for items with burden, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
For heavy goods in salam, Abu Hanifa requires explicit delivery place; Sahibayn default to contract location.

When you order a truckload of wheat to be delivered later, where must it be delivered? This passage from the Hanafi tradition tackles exactly that question in the salam contract. The authors first define what qualifies as 'having a burden'—items whose transport costs matter—then dive into an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two students on whether the place of delivery must be stated. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for drafting valid salam contracts.

Guided Path
1
Define the scope
Identify which items fall under 'himl wa mu'nah' (burden and expense). The text defines them as heavy goods requiring transport cost, like wheat and barley, as opposed to light items like musk.
This definition triggers the dispute.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, specifying the delivery place for such items is wajib; omission renders the contract fasid (void). He argues that salam is deferred, so no immediate necessity determines the place, and ambiguity leads to dispute.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that delivery is at the place of the contract by default, just as in sale, loan, etc. They rely on qiyas: the obligation to deliver arises from the contract, so the place of the contract is the natural place.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Analyze the arguments
The Sahibayn's qiyas is countered by Abu Hanifa's point that immediate delivery is not required in salam, so the analogy fails. Also, if the contract place were fixed, it could not be changed by stipulation—unlike in sale where immediate delivery is needed.
The key distinction is the deferred nature of salam.
5
Apply the dispute to analogous cases
The same ikhtilaf applies to the price (thaman) in deferred sales, rent (ujrah), and additional consideration in division (qisma) when the countervalue is heavy. In each, Abu Hanifa requires specification; the Sahibayn default to the place of contract.
This shows the principle's broad application in muamalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي إذا كان المُسَلَّمُ فيه شيئاً لحِمْلِهِ مؤنة يجب بيان مكان إيفائه عند أبي حنيفة ه، وعندهما يوفيه في مكان العقدِ الشيء، ومنه ما له جمل ومؤنة، يعنون ما له ثقل يحتاج في حمله إلى ظهر أو أجرة حَمَّاله ، وبيانه في لفظ الأصل : ما له مؤنة. وفي «المنتخب»: باري بر گردن بر وارند. انتهى. ومؤنة بالفتح : باروكراني وهي فعولة، كذا في منتهى الأرب». قيل : ما له جمل ومؤنة : هو ما يكون بحال لو أمر إنساناً يحمله إلى مجلس القضاء لا يحمله مجاناً ، وقيل : هو ما يمكن رفعه بيد واحدة ، وقيل : ما يحتاج في نقله إلى المؤنة كالحنطة والشعير، وما لا يحتاجُ فيه إليها فهو مما لا مؤنة له، كالمسك والكافور، كذا في «الكفاية»(۱). ١ ] قوله وعندهما ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ التسليم موجب للعقد، فيتعين له موضع وجوده كما في البيع ؛ ولهذا وجب تسليم رأس مالِ السَّلَم في ذلك المكان، فكذا البدلُ الآخر، إذ العقـد يـوجب المساواة ؛ لأنَّ السبب الموجب للأحكام المتعلقة به، والتسليم من جملتها. فيتعين له موضعُ وجوده دلالة ما لم يعينا له مكاناً آخر بالنص، فيتعينُ له ذلك المكان ؛ لأنه يفوق الدلالة، وبه قالت الثلاثة ؛ كبيع وقرض وإتلاف وغصب، فإنَّ مكانها يتعيَّن لتسليم المبيع والقرض وبدل المتلف والمغصوب. ولأبي حنيفة : إنَّ تعيين مكان العقدِ إمَّا بالتعيين صريحاً أو ضرورة وجوب التسليم عليه في الحال ولم يوجد ، إذ السلم لا يجوز إلا مؤجلاً ، فيكون التسليم متأخراً ضرورة، بخلاف البيع والإتلاف والقرض والغصب ؛ لأنه واجب التسليم في الحال، فيتعين موضع وجود السبب ضرورة ؛ ولأنه لو تعيَّن مكان العقد لمـا جـاز تغييره بالشرط : كمكان البيع في حقِّ العين، وكتعين مكان العقد فيما إذا عقداً في لجة البحر، وفساده لا يخفى. (۱) «الكفاية» (٦ : ٢٢٦). وعلى هذا الخلاف : الثَّمن والأجرة إذا كان لحِمْلها مؤنة، والقسمة : أي إذا اقتسما الدار، وجعلا مع نصيب أحدهما شيئاً لحمله مؤنة.
That is: if the subject matter of salam is something whose carriage has a burden, it is obligatory to specify the place of delivery according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he delivers it at the place of contract. The thing: from it is that which has burden and expense, meaning that which has weight needing a beast of burden or a porter's wage for its carriage, and its explanation in the wording of the matn: that which has expense. And in al-Muntakhab: [Persian: burden on the neck they bear]. End. And mu'nah with fatha means burden (barukrani) and it is fa'ulah, as in Muntaha al-Arab. It is said: that which has burden and expense is that which is such that if one orders a person to carry it to the court, he does not carry it for free; and it is said: it is that which can be lifted with one hand; and it is said: it is that whose transport needs expense, like wheat and barley, and that which does not need expense is that which has no burden, like musk and camphor. As in al-Kifayah (1). His statement: 'And according to the two ... etc.' because delivery is a requirement of the contract, so its place of occurrence is determined as in sale; and for this reason delivery of the capital of salam is obligatory in that place, so too the other substitute, because the contract requires equality; because the cause necessitates the rulings related to it, and delivery is among them. So its place of occurrence is determined by implication as long as they do not specify another place by explicit text, then that place is determined because it supersedes implication, and the three (Imams) said this; like sale, loan, destruction, and usurpation, for their places are determined for delivery of the sold item, loan, substitute of the destroyed, and usurped. And for Abu Hanifa: the determination of the place of contract is either by explicit specification or by necessity of immediate delivery, and it did not occur, since salam is not permissible except deferred, so delivery is deferred by necessity, unlike sale, destruction, loan, and usurpation; because delivery is obligatory immediately, so the place of the cause is determined by necessity; and because if the place of contract were determined, it would not be permissible to change it by stipulation, like the place of sale with respect to the specific item, and like the determination of the place of contract when they contract in the middle of the sea, and its corruption is not hidden. (1) Al-Kifayah (6:226). And on this dispute: the price and the rent if their carriage has a burden, and the division: that is if they divide a house and put with the share of one of them something whose carriage has a burden.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on a key condition in salam (advance payment sale): the place of delivery for items that require effort or cost to transport. The author first defines what is meant by 'has a burden and expense'—items that are heavy or bulky such that transporting them involves cost or difficulty, as opposed to light items like musk and camphor. The core of the passage is the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that specifying the place of delivery is obligatory (wajib) for the validity of the contract; if omitted, the contract is fasid (void). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that delivery is at the place of contract by default, just as in sale, loan, etc. The text then presents the reasoning of each side. The Sahibayn argue by analogy (qiyas) to sale and other contracts where delivery is immediate, so the place of contract is naturally the place of delivery. Abu Hanifa responds that salam is deferred, so there is no such necessity; moreover, if the place were fixed by default, it could not be changed by stipulation, whereas in practice parties may agree on a different location. The passage also extends this dispute to analogous cases: the price (thaman) in a deferred payment sale, rent (ujrah) in a hire contract, and additional consideration in a division (qisma) of property. In all these, if the countervalue has a burden, Abu Hanifa requires specification. This is a typical Hanafi pattern: a ruling is derived from a principle (here, the nature of deferred obligation) and then applied analogously to other transactions.

Key Terms
السلم (as-salam): An advance payment sale where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered later on a specified date.
المُسَلَّمُ فيه (al-musallam fihi): The subject matter of the salam contract; the goods to be delivered in the future.
حِمْل ومُؤْنَة (himl wa mu'nah): A burden and expense; here refers to items that are heavy or require significant cost to transport.
مكان الإيفاء (makan al-ifa'): The place of delivery; the location where the seller must deliver the musallam fihi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a salam contract for 500 kg of dates (a heavy item) does not specify the delivery place?
A) The contract is valid and delivery is at the place of contract.
B) The contract is fasid (void) due to missing condition.
C) The contract is valid but the buyer may choose the place.
D) The contract is suspended until place is agreed.

Key Takeaway
For salam items with transport burden, the Hanafi mufta bihi (Abu Hanifa) requires explicit delivery place; otherwise the contract is fasid.

Ruling on Delivery Place for Salam Items without Burden
Ruling on delivery place for lightweight salam items with intra-madhhab dispute on voluntary specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delivery place for lightweight salam items and distinguish the two narrations on voluntary specification, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
For items without burden, delivery place is not required by consensus; but if specified, it is binding to avoid transport risk.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position on delivery place in salam contracts for lightweight goods. While consensus says no specification is needed, an intra-madhhab dispute arises when parties voluntarily specify a place. The most authentic view makes it binding to prevent potential dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item category and the consensus ruling
The passage defines 'items without burden' (e.g., musk, camphor, saffron, small pearls). The consensus is that no specification of delivery place is required for the validity of salam.
The reason is that value does not differ by location.
2
Understand the rationale for no specification
Since the item's monetary value is uniform across places, ambiguity does not lead to harmful dispute. The seller may deliver anywhere he wishes.
3
Recognize the secondary issue: voluntary specification
If the parties do specify a place, does that become binding? The school has two narrations.
One says not binding (no benefit), the other says binding (al-asahh).
4
Analyze the two narrations and their reasoning
First: specification does not benefit because the seller bears no extra cost and value doesn't vary. Second: specification benefits by removing 'khatr al-tariq' (risk of the road) for the buyer.
The second reasoning prevails.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
The text states 'wa huwa al-asahh' (and it is the most authentic) – the binding view is relied upon. This is based on al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah.
Practical: eliminates potential conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وما لا حمل له ... الخ ؛ أي ما لا يكون له حمل ومؤنة كالمسك والكافور والزعفران، وصغار اللؤلؤ : أعني القليل منه لا يحتاجُ لصحة السلم فيه إلى بيان مكانِ الإيفاء بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ قيمته لا يختلفُ باختلافِ الأماكن، ويوفيه في أي مكان شاء، وهو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ الأماكن كلها سواء، ولا وجوب في الحال حتى يتعين مكان العقد. ولو عينا فيما لا حمل له مكان، قيل : لا يتعيّن ؛ لأنَّ التعيين لا يفيد شيئاً، فإنّه لا يلزمه بنقله مؤنة ، ولا يختلفُ ماليَّته باختلاف الأماكن ، وقيل : يتعين وهو الأصح ؛ لأنه يفيد سقوط خطر الطريق لربِّ السَّلَم. كذا في «الهداية»(۳)، و«العناية»، وغيرها.
[2] His statement: 'And what has no burden ... etc.'; that is, what has no burden and expense, like musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls: I mean the small amount of it, does not need, for the validity of salam, the specification of the place of delivery by consensus; because its value does not differ according to places, and he delivers it in any place he wishes, and it is the most authentic; because all places are equal, and there is no immediate obligation such that the place of contract is determined. And if they specify a place for what has no burden, it is said: it is not binding; because the specification does not benefit anything, for he is not obliged to bear the expense of transport, and its monetary value does not differ according to places. And it is said: it is binding, and it is the most authentic; because it benefits the falling of the risk of the road for the owner of the salam. As in al-Hidayah (3), al-'Inayah, and others.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage addresses salam items that have no significant burden or transport cost, such as musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls. The ruling is that no specification of delivery place is needed for the validity of the contract—there is consensus on this. The reason is that the value of such items does not vary by location, so ambiguity does not lead to harmful dispute. The seller may deliver wherever he wishes; this is the most authentic position (al-asahh). However, a secondary issue arises: if the parties do voluntarily specify a delivery place, is that specification binding? There are two narrations: one says it is not binding because it serves no purpose (no extra cost or value difference), and another says it is binding, and this is the most authentic view because it removes the risk of travel from the salam recipient. The text follows the latter, citing al-Hidayah and al-'Inayah. This shows that even where specification is not required, once agreed upon, it becomes a binding term to prevent potential dispute (to remove 'danger of the road'). This is an example of Hanafi practical reasoning: balancing necessity and consent.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of scholars that items without burden do not require specification of delivery place in salam.
الأصح (al-asahh): The most authentic or correct position among conflicting narrations within the Hanafi school.
خطر الطريق (khatr al-tariq): The risk or danger of transportation; the possibility of loss or damage during transit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the most authentic Hanafi ruling if the parties specify a delivery place for a salam contract involving saffron (an item without burden)?
A) The specification is not binding because saffron's value does not vary by place.
B) The specification is binding because it removes the risk of transport from the buyer.
C) The contract becomes invalid because saffron cannot be subject to salam.
D) The seller may ignore the specification and deliver wherever he wishes.

Key Takeaway
For salam items without transport burden, no delivery place is needed, but voluntary specification becomes binding to eliminate risk.

Place of Delivery in Salam: Goods With and Without Carriage Cost
Explaining ruling on delivery location for goods with and without carriage cost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for place specification in salam and distinguish between goods with and without carriage cost.

Core Idea
For goods with carriage cost, place must be specified; for goods without, delivery anywhere is valid.

This passage clarifies a key condition in salam contracts: specifying the place of delivery. The ruling differs based on whether the goods have carriage cost (himl). We'll examine the main positions and the qiyas behind this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Distinguish goods with carriage cost (e.g., firewood) from those without (e.g., musk, saffron). This determines the condition for validity.
The category depends on whether the goods require significant transport expense.
2
State the ruling for goods without carriage cost
There is consensus (ijma) that no place specification is needed. The seller delivers wherever he wishes.
Value does not vary by location.
3
Identify the dispute for goods with carriage cost
Abu Hanifa requires place specification as a condition (shart); the Sahibayn do not. The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
If unspecified, the contract is fasid per Abu Hanifa.
4
Understand the illa and thamarah
The illa (cause) is variation in value by location, similar to variation by quality. The practical consequence (thamarah) is whether the contract is valid when place is unspecified.
Qiyas on quality specification reinforces Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما لا حِمْلَ له يوفِّيه حيث شاء، هو الأصح
فإذا لم يتعين ولم يعيّنا مكاناً آخر بقي مجهولاً جهالة فاحشة، فيؤدي إلى المنازعة، فيفسد كاختلاف الصفة ؛ لأنَّ قيمة ما له حِمْل ومؤنةٌ تختلف باختلاف الأماكن، كما تختلف باختلافِ الصفة، ألا ترى أنَّ الحطب في المدن أغلـى مـنـه في القرى، كذا في «المنح»، وغيره.
[1] قوله : وعلى هذا الخلاف الثمن...الخ ؛ يعني إذا اشترى داراً، أو استأجرها بمكيل أو موزون موصوف في الذمة، أو اقتسماها وأخذ أحدهما أكثر من نصيبه، والتزم بمقابلة الزائد بمكيل أو موزون، كذلك إلى أجل ؛ فعنده يشترط بيان مكان الإيفاء، وهو الصحيح، وعندهما لا يشترط ، كذا في «النهر».
وقيل : لا يشترط بيان مكان الإيفاء عند الكلِّ في الثمن ، والصحيح أنه يشترط عنده لا عندهما إذا كان الثمن مؤجلاً ، واختاره شمس الأئمةِ السَّرَخْسِي ، كذا في «الهداية»(۲) وغيرها.
[٢] قوله : وما لا حمل له ... الخ ؛ أي ما لا يكون له حمل ومؤنة كالمسك والكافور والزعفران، وصغار اللؤلؤ : أعني القليل منه لا يحتاجُ لصحة السلم فيه إلى بيان مكانِ الإيفاء بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ قيمته لا يختلفُ باختلافِ الأماكن، ويوفيه في أي مكان شاء، وهو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ الأماكن كلها سواء، ولا وجوب في الحال حتى يتعين مكان العقد. ولو عينا فيما لا حمل له مكان، قيل : لا يتعيّن ؛ لأنَّ التعيين لا يفيد شيئاً، فإنّه لا يلزمه بنقله مؤنة ، ولا يختلفُ ماليَّته باختلاف الأماكن ، وقيل : يتعين وهو الأصح ؛ لأنه يفيد سقوط خطر الطريق لربِّ السَّلَم. كذا في «الهداية»(۳)، و«العناية»، وغيرها.
وفي رواية «الجامع الصغير» يوفيه مكان العقد
[١]قوله : يوفيه مكانَ العقد ؛ أي يوفِّي المُسَلَّمَ إليه ما لا حمل له في المكان الذي أسلم فيه ؛ لأنه موضع الالتزام ، فيترجح على غيره، فقوله: هو الأصح احتراز عن هذه الرواية.
And what has no carriage cost, he delivers it wherever he wishes; that is the most correct. If it is not specified and they did not specify another place, it remains unknown with gross uncertainty, leading to dispute, so it becomes invalid like difference in quality; because the value of what has carriage and cost differs by location, just as it differs by quality. Do you not see that firewood in cities is more expensive than in villages? Thus in 'Al-Munah' and others. [1] His statement: 'And upon this difference is the price... etc.' means: if he buys a house, or rents it with a measured or weighed item described in the liability, or they divide it and one takes more than his share and commits to the excess with a measured or weighed item, likewise to a term; then according to him (Abu Hanifa) specifying the place of delivery is required, and that is correct, while according to them (the two companions) it is not required. Thus in 'Al-Nahr'. And it is said: specifying place of delivery is not required according to all regarding the price, and the correct view is that it is required according to him not them when the price is deferred, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi chose this. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah' and others. [2] His statement: 'And what has no carriage cost... etc.' i.e., what has no carriage and cost like musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls: I mean the small quantity does not require, for the validity of salam, specification of the place of delivery by consensus; because its value does not differ by location, and he delivers it in any place he wishes, and that is the most correct; because all places are equal, and there is no obligation at the moment such that the place of contract is determined. And if they specify a place for what has no carriage cost, it is said: it does not become determined; because specification is of no benefit, as it does not obligate him to bear cost for transport, nor does its monetary value differ by location. And it is said: it becomes determined, and that is the most correct; because it eliminates the risk of the road for the salam buyer. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah', 'Al-Inayah', and others. And in a narration of 'Al-Jami' al-Saghir': he delivers at the place of contract. [1] His statement: 'He delivers at the place of contract'; i.e., the one to whom salam is made delivers what has no carriage cost at the place where he made the salam; because it is the place of commitment, so it outweighs others. So his statement 'that is the most correct' is to avoid this narration.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on the place of delivery in a salam contract. The author distinguishes between goods that have a 'himl' (carriage cost) and those that do not. For goods without carriage cost (like musk, saffron, small pearls), the seller may deliver anywhere he wishes, and this is the most correct (al-ashah) position. This is based on consensus (ijma) because the value does not vary by location, so there is no uncertainty. However, a variant from Al-Jami' al-Saghir says he must deliver at the place of contract; but the author notes that the statement 'that is the most correct' is meant to exclude this weak narration. For goods with carriage cost (like firewood), the place of delivery must be specified to avoid gross uncertainty (jahalah fahishah), as the value varies by location just as it does by quality. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) do not require specification, holding the contract valid without it. The text clarifies that the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's, as it is 'al-ashah'. The passage also applies this principle to similar cases: the price (thaman) in a deferred sale and the rent (ujrah) if there is carriage cost, as well as to cases of partition (qismah) where one party takes extra property and owes compensation.

Key Terms
حِمْل (himl): Carriage cost; the expense of transporting goods from one place to another.
مُؤْنَة (mu'nah): Expense or cost associated with transport or handling.
إيفاء (ifa'): Delivery of the salam goods by the seller to the buyer.
السَّلَم (al-salam): A sale where the price is paid in advance and the goods are deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the place of delivery for salam goods that have no carriage cost (like musk)?
A) They must be delivered at the place of contract
B) They must be delivered at a place agreed upon by both parties
C) The seller may deliver them wherever he wishes
D) The buyer may choose the place of delivery

Key Takeaway
For goods with carriage cost, place must be specified (Abu Hanifa, mufta bihi); for goods without, delivery is wherever the seller wishes.

The Condition of Taking Capital Before Separation in Salam
Condition for continuation of salam contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the condition of qabd in salam and correctly identify what constitutes iftiraq that invalidates the contract.

Core Idea
In salam, the capital must be taken before the parties separate; separation means being out of sight, not leaving the session.

In a salam contract, the buyer pays the capital in advance. For the contract to remain valid, the seller must take possession of that capital before the two parties separate. This lesson unpacks that condition, clarifies what 'separation' means according to the Hanafi school, and explores various scenarios involving defective or counterfeit capital.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Locate the key phrase 'قبض رأس المال قبل الافتراق' and understand that it is a condition for the salam contract to remain in effect, not just for its initial validity.
The condition applies after the contract is made; if not fulfilled, the contract is rescinded.
2
Understand the definition of iftiraq
Examine the text's clarification that iftiraq means one party becomes hidden from the sight of the other, not merely leaving the location. Walking together before possession does not break the contract.
This distinguishes Hanafi view from other schools that may define separation by leaving the majlis.
3
Apply to scenarios of defective capital
Work through the examples: if capital is defective, counterfeit, or of wrong type, the buyer may reject it. Exchange after separation invalidates unless accepted in the same session or later approved.
The ruling depends on whether the defect is in genus (e.g., lead vs silver) or in quality (e.g., counterfeit).
4
Determine the consequence of omission
If the parties separate without the seller taking possession of the capital, the contract becomes batil (rescinded). This is a mufsid (invalidator) of the contract.
No compensation is mentioned; the contract simply dissolves.

ثُمَّ لَمَّا فَرَغَ من بيانِ شروطِهِ صِحَّةِ السَّلَم ذَكَرَ شَرْطَ بقائه ، فقال : (وقبض رأس المال) قبل الافتراق
[٢] قوله : قبض رأس المال ... الخ ؛ فلو انتقض القبضُ بطل السَّلَمُ كما لو كان عيناً فوجده معيباً أو مستحقاً، ولم يرض بالعيب أو لم يجز المستحق أو ديناً فاستحق، ولم يجزه، واستبدل بعد المجلس، فلو قبله صح، أو وجده زيوفاً أو نَبَهْرَجَة، وردُّها بعد الافتراق سواء استبدلها في مجلس الرد أو لا.
فلو قبله واستبدلها في المجلس، أو رضي بها ، ولـو بعـد الافتراق صح، والكثير كالكل، وفي تحديدِهِ :روايتان ما زادَ على الثلاث أو ما زادَ على النصف، وإن وجده ستوقة أو رصاصاً، فإن استبدلها في المجلس صح ، وإن بعد الافتراق بطل، وإن رضي بها ؛ لأنها غير جنس حقه، كذا في «البحر»(۳)، وغيره.
[۳] قوله : قبل الافتراق ؛ أي قبل أن يفارق كلُّ واحدٍ من المتعاقدين صاحبه بدناً لا مكاناً، حتى لو مشيا فرسخاً قبل القبض لم يفسد ما لم يتفرقا من غير قبض ، فإن افترقا كذلك فسد. فالمراد بالافتراقِ أن يتوارى أحدهما عن عين صاحبه حتى لو دخل ربُّ السَّلَم بيته لإخراج الدراهم ولم يغب عن عين صاحبه لا يكون افتراقاً، وإنما قال: قبل الافتراق، ولم يقل : في المجلس ؛ لأنَّ القبض في المجلس ليس بشرط ، حتى لو تعاقدا
Then when he finished explaining the conditions of the validity of salam, he mentioned a condition for its continuation, and said: 'And taking possession of the capital before separation.' [2] His statement: 'Taking possession of the capital... etc.'; so if possession is invalidated, the salam is rescinded, as if it were a specific item and he found it defective or belonging to someone else, and he did not accept the defect or did not permit the rightful owner, or it was a debt and it was claimed by another, and he did not permit it, and he exchanged after the session, then if he accepted it, it is valid; or he found it to be counterfeit or unmarketable, and he returned it after separation whether he exchanged it in the session of return or not. So if he accepted it and exchanged it in the session, or was pleased with it even after separation, it is valid. And the large quantity is like the whole, and regarding its definition: there are two narrations: what exceeds three or what exceeds half. And if he found it to be base metal or lead, then if he exchanged it in the session, it is valid; if after separation, it is invalid, even if he was pleased with it; because it is not of the same type as his due. Thus in 'Al-Bahr' and others. [3] His statement: 'before separation'; i.e., before each of the two contracting parties physically separates from his companion, not by location, such that even if they walk a farsakh before possession, it is not invalidated as long as they do not separate without possession. If they separate thus, it is invalidated. So what is meant by separation is that one of them becomes hidden from the sight of the other, such that if the salam buyer enters his house to bring out the dirhams and does not disappear from the sight of his companion, it is not separation. And he said 'before separation' and did not say 'in the session' because taking possession in the session is not a condition; even if they contract
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn clause: 'And taking possession of the capital before separation' – which is a condition for the salam contract to remain valid. The author explains that if the capital (ra's al-mal) is not taken by the buyer before the parties separate (afitraaq), the contract is rescinded (batal). He details various scenarios: if the capital is a specific item and found defective or belonging to another, the buyer may reject it, and if he exchanges it after separation without acceptance, the contract is invalid. If he accepts it and exchanges in the same session or is pleased later, it is valid. For counterfeit or unmarketable coins, if the buyer exchanges them in the session, valid; after separation, invalid. For base metal (like lead), it is not of the same genus, so exchange after separation invalidates. The text also defines 'afitraaq' as physical separation such that one party is out of sight of the other, not merely leaving the location. This is important: the condition is 'before afitraaq', not 'in the majlis', because possession can occur before separation even if not in the same session. The commentary relies on Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and other sources.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the capital in a salam contract.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or price paid in advance by the buyer in a salam contract.
افتراق (iftiraq): Separation of the two contracting parties such that one becomes hidden from the sight of the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what constitutes 'afitraaq' (separation) that invalidates the salam contract if قبض has not occurred?
A) Leaving the physical location of the contract
B) One party disappearing from the sight of the other
C) A verbal declaration of separation
D) Walking more than a mile together

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi salam, the capital must be taken before the parties separate; separation means one party is out of sight of the other.

Condition of Taking Possession of Capital Before Separation in Salam
Explains condition of qabd before iftiraq for salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires possession of the capital (qabd) before separation (iftiraq) in a salam contract, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving defects and mixed capital.

Core Idea
A salam contract becomes void if parties separate without the seller taking possession of the capital — separation means one disappears from the other's sight.

In this lesson, we examine a critical condition for the validity of a salam (forward sale) contract: the seller must take possession of the capital (ra's al-mal) before the parties physically separate. We will see that this condition is for the contract's continued validity, not its formation, and that it applies to both determinable and non-determinable capital. The passage also covers what happens if the capital is defective, counterfeit, or mixed with a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking possession of capital (qabd) in salam. The ruling: it is a condition (shart) for the contract to remain valid. Without it, the contract becomes void (fasid) upon separation.
This is a condition of survival (baqa'), not conclusion (in'iqad).
2
Distinguish condition for conclusion vs. survival
The salam contract initially concludes validly even without qabd. It becomes void only if separation occurs before qabd. This is a key Hanafi distinction.
3
Define key terms: qabd and iftiraq
Qabd: taking physical or constructive possession. Iftiraq: physical separation such that one party disappears from the sight of the other, not merely spatial distance.
Walking together does not count as separation; entering a house while visible does not count.
4
Apply to scenarios
If the capital is found defective, counterfeit, or of a different metal, the ruling depends on whether the buyer accepts or changes it, and whether this happens in the session or after separation.
If capital is mixed cash and debt, only the debt portion becomes void because it is a debt-for-debt exchange.
5
Identify the proof
The prohibition of bai' al-kali' bil-kali' (sale of credit for credit) and the rationale that salam must have immediate capital to match deferred delivery.
The hadith 'بيع الكالي بالكالئ منهي عنه' is cited as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب السلم
وقبض رأس المال قبل الافتراق
وفي رواية «الجامع الصغير» يوفيه مكان العقد
ثُمَّ لَمَّا فَرَغَ من بيانِ شروطِهِ صِحَّةِ السَّلَم ذَكَرَ شَرْطَ بقائه ، فقال :
(وقبض رأس المال) قبل الافتراق
[١]قوله : يوفيه مكانَ العقد ؛ أي يوفِّي المُسَلَّمَ إليه ما لا حمل له في المكان الذي أسلم فيه ؛ لأنه موضع الالتزام ، فيترجح على غيره، فقوله: هو الأصح احتراز عن هذه الرواية.
[٢] قوله : قبض رأس المال ... الخ ؛ فلو انتقض القبضُ بطل السَّلَمُ كما لو كان عيناً فوجده معيباً أو مستحقاً، ولم يرض بالعيب أو لم يجز المستحق أو ديناً فاستحق، ولم يجزه، واستبدل بعد المجلس، فلو قبله صح، أو وجده زيوفاً أو نَبَهْرَجَة، وردُّها بعد الافتراق سواء استبدلها في مجلس الرد أو لا.
فلو قبله واستبدلها في المجلس، أو رضي بها ، ولـو بعـد الافتراق صح، والكثير كالكل، وفي تحديدِهِ :روايتان ما زادَ على الثلاث أو ما زادَ على النصف، وإن وجده ستوقة أو رصاصاً، فإن استبدلها في المجلس صح ، وإن بعد الافتراق بطل، وإن رضي بها ؛ لأنها غير جنس حقه، كذا في «البحر»، وغيره.
[٣] قوله : قبل الافتراق ؛ أي قبل أن يفارق كلُّ واحدٍ من المتعاقدين صاحبه بدناً لا مكاناً، حتى لو مشيا فرسخاً قبل القبض لم يفسد ما لم يتفرقا من غير قبض ، فإن افترقا كذلك فسد. فالمراد بالافتراقِ أن يتوارى أحدهما عن عين صاحبه حتى لو دخل ربُّ السَّلَم بيته لإخراج الدراهم ولم يغب عن عين صاحبه لا يكون افتراقاً، وإنما قال: قبل الافتراق، ولم يقل : في المجلس ؛ لأنَّ القبض في المجلس ليس بشرط ، حتى لو تعاقدا السلم، ولم يغب أحدهما عن صاحبه يوماً أو يومين، ثمَّ سَلَّم رأس المال صح التسليم. كما صرحوا به.
[١]قوله : شرط بقائه ؛ أي شرط بقاء السلم على الصحة، لا شرط انعقاده ؛ لأنَّ السَّلَمَ ينعقد صحيحاً بدونِهِ ثم يفسد بالافتراق قبل القبض، ولا فرق بين كون رأس المال ما يتعيَّن كالنقود، أو مما لا يتعيَّن كالعروض.
أما الأول ؛ فلئلا يفترقا عن دين بدين، وهو بيع الكالي بالكالئ، وهو منهي عنه. وأما الثاني : فلأنَّ السَّلَمَ أخذ عاجل بأجل ، والمسلَّم فيه أجل، فوجب أن يكونَ رأس المال عاجلاً ؛ ليكون حكمُهُ على وفق اسمه، ولأنَّه لا بُدَّ من تسليم رأس المال إلى المسلم إليه ؛ ليتصرّف فيه، فيقدر على تسليم المُسَلَّم فيه، كذا في «كمال الدراية» وغيره.
[٢] قوله : على المسلّم إليه ؛ وإنّما قال: ديناً على المسلم ؛ لأنه لو كان الدين على الأجنبي فهو غير صحيح في حق الكل، حتى لو نقداً من ماله في المجلس، لم ينقلب إليه جائزاً، بخلاف ما إذا كان الدَّينُ على المسلَّم إليه فإنّه بالنقدِ في المجلس ينقلب إلى الجواز.
(۳) قوله : في كُرّ ؛ الكُر بضم الكاف وتشديد الراء : ستون قفيراً، والقفيز ثمانية مكاكيك، والمكوك صاع ونصف ، وقيل : الكرُّ أربعون قفيز. كذا في «المنح».
(٤) قوله : بطل في حصة الدين فقط ؛ لأنه دين بدين، وصح في حصة النقد لوجود قبض رأس المال في المجلس، سواءً كان العقد مطلقاً، بأن قال : أسلمتُ إليك مئتي درهم في كرّ حنطة، ثم جعلا مئة من رأس المال تقاصا بالدين، أو مقيداً، بأن قال : أسلمتُ إليك في مئة نقد، ومئةٍ دين لي عليك ، وسواء أضيف إلى دراهم بعينها أو لا.
Book of Sale, Chapter of Salam. And taking possession of capital before separation. And in a narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir: he gives it in the place of the contract. Then when he finished explaining its conditions of validity of salam, he mentioned the condition of its survival, and said: (And taking possession of capital) before separation. [1] His saying: he gives it in the place of the contract; meaning the one receiving salam pays in the place where he made the salam because it is the place of obligation, so it is preferred over others. So his saying: it is the most correct is a precaution against this narration. [2] His saying: taking possession of capital... etc.; so if possession is invalidated, the salam becomes void, as if it was a specific item and he found it defective or claimed by another, and he did not accept the defect or did not validate the claim, or it was a debt and it was claimed and he did not validate it, and he changed it after the session. If he accepted it then it is valid, or he found it counterfeit or debased and returned it after separation, whether he changed it in the session of return or not. If he accepted it and changed it in the session, or was satisfied with it even after separation, it is valid; and the majority is like the whole; regarding its definition there are two narrations: what exceeds three or what exceeds half. If he found it of bad silver or lead, then if he changes it in the session it is valid, but if after separation it is void; and if he is satisfied with it because it is not of the same genus as his due, as in al-Bahr and others. [3] His saying: before separation; meaning before each of the contracting parties separates from his companion physically not spatially; even if they walk a parasang before possession it does not become void as long as they have not separated without taking possession; if they separate like that then it becomes void. The meaning of separation is that one disappears from the sight of his companion; even if the owner of salam enters his house to fetch the dirhams and does not disappear from his companion's sight it is not separation. He said 'before separation' and did not say 'in the session' because taking possession in the session is not a condition; even if they contract salam and one does not disappear from his companion's sight for a day or two, then delivers the capital, the delivery is valid, as they explicitly stated. [1] His saying: condition of its survival; meaning condition of the salam's continued validity, not condition of its conclusion; because the salam is valid without it, then becomes void by separation before possession. There is no difference whether the capital is determinable like money or non-determinable like goods. As for the first: so that they do not separate on a debt for a debt, which is the sale of credit for credit, and it is prohibited. As for the second: because salam is taking a present for a future, and the subject matter is future, so the capital must be present so that its ruling conforms to its name, and because the capital must be delivered to the seller so that he can use it and thus be able to deliver the subject matter, as in Kamal al-Dirayah and others. [2] His saying: to the seller; and he only said 'debt to the seller' because if the debt is from a third party, it is invalid in all respects, even if he pays cash from his own money in the session, it does not become valid, unlike if the debt is on the seller, then by paying cash in the session it becomes valid. (3) His saying: in a kurr; al-kurr with damma on kaf and tashdid on ra is sixty qafiz, and a qafiz is eight makakik, and a makkuk is one and a half sa', and it is said: kurr is forty qafiz. As in al-Minhaj. (4) His saying: it becomes void only in the portion of the debt; because it is debt for debt, and it is valid in the portion of cash due to the existence of taking possession of capital in the session, whether the contract was absolute, by saying 'I have sold to you two hundred dirhams for a kurr of wheat' then they made one hundred of the capital from the debt, or restricted, by saying 'I have sold to you for one hundred cash and one hundred that is a debt I have on you', and whether it is added to specific dirhams or not.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Sale, Chapter of Salam, explaining the condition that the buyer (musallim) must take possession of the capital (ra's al-mal) before the parties separate (iftiraq). The author explains that this is a condition for the validity of the salam contract, not for its conclusion; the contract is initially valid but becomes void if they separate without possession. The passage then details various scenarios: if the capital is found defective or claimed by another, the ruling depends on whether the buyer accepts or changes it; if it is counterfeit (zuyuf/nabarhajah), changing it in the session or accepting it after separation is valid; if it is of a different metal (siyaq/rasas), changing it after separation voids the contract. The key definition of iftiraq is physical separation where one party cannot see the other; merely walking together or entering a house while visible does not count. The author then gives the rationale: to prevent a debt-for-debt sale (bai' al-kali' bil-kali'), which is prohibited by hadith, and to ensure that the salam remains a present-for-future transaction. Finally, an example is given where the capital is part cash and part debt: only the debt portion becomes void because it is a debt-for-debt exchange, while the cash portion remains valid due to possession in the session.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the capital (ra's al-mal) by the seller (musallam ilayh) in a salam contract.
افتراق (iftiraq): Physical separation of the contracting parties such that one disappears from the sight of the other; not mere spatial distance.
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price (ra's al-mal) is paid upfront for a deferred delivery of a specified commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, what is the correct definition of 'separation' (iftiraq) as used in the condition of qabd?
A) The parties ending their verbal negotiations.
B) One party disappearing from the sight of the other.
C) The parties walking more than a certain distance.
D) The contract being signed.

Key Takeaway
The condition of qabd before iftiraq in salam prevents a debt-for-debt sale; physical separation without possession voids the contract, but possession within the session can be delayed as long as the parties don't lose sight of each other.

Condition of Immediate Delivery of Capital in Salam
Ruling on capital being cash in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on capital in salam when part is debt, apply the distinction between debt owed by the seller versus a third party, and explain why supervenient invalidity does not spread to the valid portion.

Core Idea
In salam, capital must be immediate; a debt portion is invalid but does not void the cash portion.

This lesson examines a key condition for the validity of salam (forward sale): the capital must be delivered immediately in the sitting. When part of the capital is a debt owed by the seller, that debt portion is invalid because it constitutes a prohibited sale of debt for debt (kālī bil kālī). However, the cash portion remains valid if paid on the spot. The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, holds that the invalidity is supervenient and does not spread, contrary to Zufar's objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the legal effect when a salam contract involves both cash and debt as capital: the debt portion is invalid (bāṭil), but the cash portion remains valid (ṣaḥīḥ).
The text specifies 'batala fī ḥiṣṣat al-dayn faqaṭ' (invalid only in the debt portion).
2
Locate the dalīl
The prohibition of bay‘ al-kālī bil kālī (selling debt for debt) is the proof. Identify the hadith and its implication that both countervalues cannot be deferred.
The hadith is a Sunnah aḥad but its meaning (dalāla) is qaṭ‘ī for prohibition.
3
Distinguish the source of the debt
Understand the difference between debt owed by the seller (which can be cured by payment in the sitting) and debt owed by a third party (which cannot be cured, voiding the whole contract).
If debt is on a third party, even later payment does not cure the defect.
4
Examine Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that partial invalidity should void the entire contract. The Hanafi response is that the invalidity is supervenient (ṭāri’) and does not spread, because the contract was initially valid.
This is based on the principle that a supervenient defect does not invalidate the original valid part.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The majority view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is the relied-upon position: the cash portion stands, the debt portion is void.
This facilitates transactions by preserving valid parts.

Source Text

شرط بقائه : فلو أسْلَمَ مئة نقداً، ومئة ديناً على المسلم إليه في كر بر بطل في حصة الدين فقط

[1] قوله : شرط بقائه ؛ أي شرط بقاء السلم على الصحة، لا شرط انعقاده ؛ لأنَّ السَّلَمَ ينعقد صحيحاً بدونِهِ ثم يفسد بالافتراق قبل القبض، ولا فرق بين كون رأس المال ما يتعيَّن كالنقود، أو مما لا يتعيَّن كالعروض. أما الأول ؛ فلئلا يفترقا عن دين بدين، وهو بيع الكالي بالكالئ، وهو منهي عنه. وأما الثاني : فلأنَّ السَّلَمَ أخذ عاجل بأجل ، والمسلَّم فيه أجل، فوجب أن يكونَ رأس المال عاجلاً ؛ ليكونَ حكمُهُ على وفق اسمه، ولأنَّه لا بُدَّ من تسليم رأس المال إلى المسلم إليه ؛ ليتصرّف فيه، فيقدر على تسليم المُسَلَّم فيه.

[2] قوله : على المسلّم إليه ؛ وإنّما قال: ديناً على المسلم ؛ لأنه لو كان الدين على الأجنبي فهو غير صحيح في حق الكل، حتى لو نقداً من ماله في المجلس، لم ينقلب إليه جائزاً، بخلاف ما إذا كان الدَّينُ على المسلَّم إليه فإنّه بالنقدِ في المجلس ينقلب إلى الجواز. (3) قوله : في كُرّ ؛ الكُر بضم الكاف وتشديد الراء : ستون قفيراً، والقفيز ثمانية مكاكيك، والمكوك صاع ونصف ، وقيل : الكرُّ أربعون قفيز. (4) قوله : بطل في حصة الدين فقط ؛ لأنه دين بدين، وصح في حصة النقد لوجود قبض رأس المال في المجلس، سواءً كان العقد مطلقاً، بأن قال : أسلمتُ إليك مئتي درهم في كرّ حنطة، ثم جعلا مئة من رأس المال تقاصا بالدين، أو مقيداً، بأن قال : أسلمتُ إليك في مئة نقد، ومئةٍ دين لي عليك ، وسواء أضيف إلى دراهم بعينها أو لا.

[1] قوله : أي لا يشيع الفساد...الخ ؛ جواب عن ما قال زفر له من أنه يشيع الفساد، ويبطل العقد في حصّةِ العين أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ هذا الفساد في صلب العقد، وتقرير الجواب أنَّ هذا الفساد طارئ فلا يشيع. أما إذا أطلق فظاهر ؛ لأنَّ عقدَ السَّلَمِ صح ابتداءً لخلوه . عن المفسدات، إذ قبض رأس المال شرط لبقاء صحة العقدِ لا لصحته، وهذا لو نقد المئتين قبل الافتراق صح، والفساد الطارئ لا يشيع عند الكل. وأما إذا أضافه إلى المئتين؛ فلأنَّ النقود في العقود لا تتعين ديناً كانت أو عيناً، حتى لـو بـاع عـيـنـاً بدين ثم تصادقا أن لا دين لم يبطل البيع إلا إذا كانا يعلمـان عـدم الدين، فيفسد لأمر آخر، وهو أنهما هازلان بالبيع، حيث عقد مبينا بلا ثمن، وإذا لم تتعين النقود كان التقييد مثل الإطلاق.

(2) قوله : لا يمنع تمامه ؛ أي تمام القبض ؛ لأنَّ تمامه تمام الصفقة، وتمامها لا يكون إلا بتمام الرضاء، وهو موجود وقت العقد، فلو أسقط خيار الشرط قبل الافتراق. ولا بد هاهنا من قيد آخر، وهو قيام رأس المال في يد المسلم إليه ؛ لأنهما لو أسقطاه بعد إنفاقه واستهلاكه لا يعود صحيحاً اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه بالاستهلاكِ صار ديناً في ذمة المسلم إليه، فلو صح كان رأس مال هو دين ، وذلك لا يجوز في ابتداء العقد.

[٣]قوله : صح خلافاً لزفر له ؛ وقد مرَّ نظيره في «باب البيع الفاسد»، وهو ما إذا باع إلى أجل مجهول ، ثم أسقطاهُ قبل حلوله ينقلب جائزاً عندنا، خلافاً لزفره ، وتقرير الاستدلال مذكور ثمة فلتطالعه.
Condition of its remaining: if one makes a salam contract for one hundred (dirhams) cash and one hundred as a debt owed by the seller in a kurr of wheat, it becomes invalid only in the portion of the debt. [1] His statement: condition of its remaining; i.e., condition for the salam to remain valid, not a condition for its conclusion; because the salam contract becomes valid without it, then becomes invalid by separation before delivery. There is no difference whether the capital is determinate like currency or non-determinate like goods. As for the first: so that they do not separate on a debt for a debt, which is the sale of credit for credit, which is prohibited. As for the second: because salam takes an immediate (payment) for a deferred (commodity), and the subject matter is deferred, so the capital must be immediate so that its ruling corresponds to its name, and because it is necessary to deliver the capital to the seller so that he may transact with it and be able to deliver the subject matter. [2] His statement: owed by the seller; he said a debt owed by the seller because if the debt is owed by a third party, it is invalid with respect to everyone, such that even if he pays it from his own wealth in the sitting, it does not become valid, contrary to the case where the debt is on the seller, because by paying it in the sitting it becomes valid. (3) His statement: in a kurr; al-kurr: sixty qafiz, and a qafiz is eight makakik, and a makkuk is one and a half sa', and it is said the kurr is forty qafiz. (4) His statement: becomes invalid only in the portion of the debt; because it is debt for debt, and it is valid in the portion of the cash due to receipt of capital in the sitting, whether the contract is absolute, or restricted. [1] His statement: i.e., the invalidity does not spread; a response to Zufar's claim that the invalidity spreads and voids the contract in the portion of the commodity as well because this invalidity is in the essence of the contract, and the answer is that this invalidity is supervenient and does not spread. As for the absolute case, it is apparent; because the salam contract was valid initially free from invalidators, since receipt of capital is a condition for the remaining validity not for its validity, and if he pays the two hundred before separation it is valid, and supervenient invalidity does not spread according to all. As for the restricted case because currencies in contracts are not determinate whether debt or specific, such that if one sells a specific item for a debt then they agree there is no debt, the sale is not invalid unless they knew of the absence of debt, then it is invalid for another reason. (2) His statement: does not prevent its complete; i.e., complete delivery; because its complete is the complete transaction, and its complete only occurs with complete consent, which exists at the time of contract, so if one annuls the option of stipulation before separation... and here another condition is necessary: the capital must still be in the hand of the seller; because if they annul after he has spent it and consumed it, it does not become valid by consensus; because by consumption it becomes a debt in the seller's liability, so if valid, the capital would be a debt, and that is not permissible at the outset of the contract. [3] His statement: valid, contrary to Zufar; and its parallel has passed in chapter of void sale, which is if one sells with an unknown term, then they annul it before its maturity, it becomes valid according to us, contrary to Zufar; the reasoning is mentioned there.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding the condition for the validity of a salam contract. The key ruling is that if part of the capital in a salam contract is a debt owed by the seller (the recipient of the capital), then that portion of the contract is invalid because it constitutes a sale of debt for debt (kali' bil kali'), which is prohibited by the Prophet ﷺ. However, the contract remains valid for the portion that is paid in cash, provided the cash is delivered in the sitting before the parties separate. The reasoning is that in salam, the capital must be immediate (عاجل) because the contract is 'immediate for deferred.' If the capital were a debt, the contract would become a deferred exchange, defeating the purpose of salam. The commentator explains that if the debt is owed by a third party (not the seller), the contract is entirely invalid and cannot be cured by later payment. But if the debt is owed by the seller, it can become valid if paid in the sitting before separation, thereby turning the capital into cash. The commentator also addresses Zufar's objection that the invalidity should spread to the entire contract, including the cash portion. The response is that the invalidity is supervenient (طارئ) because the contract was initially valid when made; the defect only arises from the failure to deliver the debt portion (since debt cannot be delivered) and is cured for the cash part because it is delivered immediately. This is based on the Hanafi principle that supervenient invalidity does not spread to the valid part. The text also discusses the effect of options (khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'ya, khiyar al-'ayb) on the salam contract: options that prevent complete delivery (like khiyar al-shart) are not allowed initially because they delay the receipt, but if the option is annulled before separation, the contract becomes valid, contrary to Zufar. Khiyar al-'ayb does not prevent complete delivery because the defect is discovered after the contract and does not hinder immediate possession.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the buyer pays the price immediately (the capital) for a specifically described commodity to be delivered later.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital (price) paid by the buyer in the salam contract. It must be a tangible asset delivered immediately in the sitting.
الكالي بالكالئ (al-kālī bi al-kālī): The exchange of debt for debt, i.e., both countervalues are deferred obligations. This is prohibited in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person pays 100 dirhams cash and owes 100 dirhams to the seller in a salam contract for wheat, what is the ruling according to the majority Hanafi position?
A) The entire contract is invalid because of debt for debt.
B) The contract is valid for 100 dirhams and the corresponding half of the wheat, with the debt portion cancelled.
C) The contract is valid only if the debt is written off before separation.
D) The contract is valid only if the buyer also delivers 100 dirhams worth of some other commodity.

Key Takeaway
In salam, capital must be immediate; if part is a debt owed by the seller, only that portion is invalid, and the supervenient defect does not void the cash portion.

Khiyar al-Shart and Khiyar al-Ru'ya in Salam: The Dispute with Zufar
Commentary on options in salam and intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that khiyar al-shart invalidates a salam contract but dropping it before separation validates it, and evaluate Zufar's counter-argument.

Core Idea
In salam, options that prevent immediate delivery invalidate the contract, but incidental corruption does not spread.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) and khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight) in salam contracts. While these options generally invalidate salam because they prevent complete delivery of capital, a key dispute emerges with Zufar over whether dropping the option before separation can cure the contract. We will walk through the reasoning of the Hanafi masters and the practical consequence of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general rule: salam is invalid with khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-ru'ya because they prevent complete delivery of capital. Also note the exception: khiyar al-ayb does not prevent delivery, so salam with it is valid.
The text states: 'السَّلَم لا يجوز مع خيارِ الشَّرط وخيارِ الرُّؤية'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar holds that the corruption caused by the option is not incidental but inherent in the contract substance, so it spreads to invalidate the entire contract irreparably. Therefore, dropping the option later cannot validate it.
Zufar's view: 'يشيع الفساد، ويبطل العقد في حصّةِ العين أيضاً'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Zufar argues that since the condition (option) is part of the contract from the start, its corruption taints the whole contract, including the commodity's share.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the corruption is incidental (tāriʼ), not in the substance (ṣalb) of the contract. The contract is valid initially; the condition only affects continuity. Receipt of capital is a condition for continuity, not validity. Therefore, dropping the condition perfects the contract.
The text says: 'هذا الفساد طارئ فلا يشيع'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
According to Abu Hanifa, if khiyar al-shart is dropped before the parties separate and the capital is still in the hands of the musallam ilayh, the contract becomes valid. According to Zufar, it remains invalid regardless.
The text adds a condition: the capital must not have been consumed, otherwise it would become a debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي لا يشيع الفساد ؛ لأن العقد صحيح وهذا الشَّرط شرط البقاء فيكون ضعيفاً. ثُمَّ من تفاريع قبض رأس المالِ أن السَّلَم لا يجوز مع خيارِ الشَّرط وخيارِ الرُّؤية ؛ لأنَّهما يَمْنَعانِ تمامَ التَّسليم بخلافِ خيار العيب ، فإِنَّه لا يَمْنَعُ تمامه، فلو أُسْقِطَ خيارُ الشَّرط قبل الافتراق صح خلافاً لزُفَرَ الله.

قوله : أي لا يشيع الفساد...الخ ؛ جواب عن ما قال زفر له من أنه يشيع الفساد، ويبطل العقد في حصّةِ العين أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ هذا الفساد في صلب العقد، وتقرير الجواب أنَّ هذا الفساد طارئ فلا يشيع.
أما إذا أطلق فظاهر ؛ لأنَّ عقدَ السَّلَمِ صح ابتداءً لخلوه عن المفسدات، إذ قبض رأس المال شرط لبقاء صحة العقدِ لا لصحته، وهذا لو نقد المئتين قبل الافتراق صح، والفساد الطارئ لا يشيع عند الكل.
وأما إذا أضافه إلى المئتين؛ فلأنَّ النقود في العقود لا تتعين ديناً كانت أو عيناً، حتى لـو بـاع عـيـنـاً بدين ثم تصادقا أن لا دين لم يبطل البيع إلا إذا كانا يعلمـان عـدم الدين، فيفسد لأمر آخر، وهو أنهما هازلان بالبيع، حيث عقد مبينا بلا ثمن، وإذا لم تتعين النقود كان التقييد مثل الإطلاق.
(۲) قوله : لا يمنع تمامه ؛ أي تمام القبض ؛ لأنَّ تمامه تمام الصفقة، وتمامها لا يكون إلا بتمام الرضاء، وهو موجود وقت العقد، فلو أسقط خيار الشرط قبل الافتراق. ولا بد هاهنا من قيد آخر، وهو قيام رأس المال في يد المسلم إليه ؛ لأنهما لو أسقطاه بعد إنفاقه واستهلاكه لا يعود صحيحاً اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه بالاستهلاكِ صار ديناً في ذمة المسلم إليه، فلو صح كان رأس مال هو دين ، وذلك لا يجوز في ابتداء العقد، كذا في «الفتح».
[٣]قوله : صح خلافاً لزفره ؛ وقد مرَّ نظيره في «باب البيع الفاسد»، وهو ما إذا باع إلى أجل مجهول ، ثم أسقطاهُ قبل حلوله ينقلب جائزاً عندنا، خلافاً لزفره ، وتقرير الاستدلال مذكور ثمة فلتطالعه.
It means: corruption does not spread because the contract is valid and this condition is a condition of continuity, so it is weak. Then from the branches of receiving the capital: Salam is not permissible with khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya because they prevent complete delivery, unlike khiyar al-ayb, which does not prevent its completion. So if khiyar al-shart is dropped before parting, it is valid, contrary to Zufar.

His statement: 'It means corruption does not spread...etc.'; it is a response to what Zufar said that it spreads corruption and invalidates the contract in the share of the commodity also because this corruption is in the substance of the contract. The response summary: this corruption is incidental so it does not spread.
As for when it is absolute, it is apparent because the salam contract is valid initially due to being free of corrupting factors, since receipt of capital is a condition for the continuity of the contract's validity, not for its validity. So if he paid the two hundred before parting, it is valid, and incidental corruption does not spread according to all.
As for when he adds it to the two hundred: because currencies in contracts are not specified, whether debt or commodity. Thus, if he sold a commodity for a debt then they agreed that there is no debt, the sale is not invalidated unless they knew of the non-existence of the debt; then it is invalid for another reason: that they are joking in the sale, since they made a contract without a price. And when currencies are not specified, the restriction is like the absolute.
(2) His statement: 'It does not prevent its completion'; i.e., complete receipt because its completion is completion of the transaction, and its completion is only with complete consent, which exists at the time of the contract. So if khiyar al-shart is dropped before parting... And here another restriction is necessary: that the capital remains in the hand of the person to whom salam is made; because if they dropped it after he spent and consumed it, it does not become valid by agreement; because by consumption it becomes a debt in the liability of the musallam ilayh. If it were valid, the capital would be a debt, and that is not permissible in the initiation of the contract. Thus in al-Fath.
[3] His statement: 'It is valid contrary to Zufar'; and its analogous case has passed in the chapter of invalid sale, which is if he sold with an unknown term, then they dropped it before its due, it becomes permissible according to us, contrary to Zufar. The exposition of the argument is mentioned there, so let him look it up.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding khiyar (options) in salam contracts. It begins by explaining that a corrupting condition in a salam contract does not spread to invalidate the entire contract because the condition is only for the continuity of the contract, not its essence. The author then states that salam is not valid with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) or khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight) because these options prevent complete delivery of the capital. In contrast, khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) does not prevent delivery, so salam with khiyar al-ayb is valid. If khiyar al-shart is dropped before the parties separate, the contract becomes valid, according to Abu Hanifa, but Zufar disagrees. The commentary elaborates on Zufar's objection that the corruption is inherent in the contract substance and thus should spread. The response is that the corruption is incidental, not integral, so it does not spread. The passage also discusses cases where the capital sum is specified (e.g., 200 dirhams) and clarifies that specifying the currency does not change the ruling because currencies are not considered specific in contracts. A key condition for the validity of dropping khiyar al-shart is that the capital must still be in the possession of the musallam ilayh; if it has been spent, the contract cannot be validated because the capital would become a debt, which is impermissible at the outset of a salam contract. The commentary also references a similar ruling in the chapter of invalid sales regarding an unknown term.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated by either party to cancel the contract within a specified period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): An option to cancel the contract upon seeing the item if it is not as described.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): An option to cancel the contract due to a defect in the item discovered after the contract.
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the buyer pays the full price upfront for a commodity to be delivered later.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or principal paid upfront in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what must be true for dropping khiyar al-shart to validate a salam contract?
A) The capital must still be in the hands of the musallam ilayh
B) The parties must recite a new contract
C) The price must be increased
D) The delivery period must be extended

Key Takeaway
In salam contracts, khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya invalidate due to preventing delivery, but dropping them before separation with capital intact validates the contract, as the corruption is incidental.

Prohibition of Disposing Capital and Musallam Fihi Before Receipt
Ruling on disposal of capital and goods in salam before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disposal of salam capital and musallam fihi before receipt and explain the proof from the hadith and the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
Disposal of salam capital or goods before possession is haram: it forfeits a sharʿi right and the goods are like a sold item.

In a salam contract, the buyer pays the full price upfront for future delivery of a specified commodity. Once the contract is concluded, the buyer may be tempted to dispose of either the capital (raʾs al-mal) or the expected goods (musallam fihi) before taking possession. This lesson explains why the Hanafi school considers such disposal prohibited (ḥarām) and how the ruling is derived from a prophetic hadith and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is disposing of the capital (ra's al-mal) or the musallam fihi (goods to be delivered) before taking physical possession. The ruling is ḥarām (prohibited).
Note that the prohibition applies to both the capital and the goods.
2
Understand the rationale for the capital
Disposing of the capital forfeits the right of receipt that is due by the contract before the parties separate. This right is a right of the Sharīʿah (ḥaqq al-sharʿ), not a personal right, so it cannot be waived.
3
Understand the rationale for the musallam fihi
The musallam fihi is treated as a sold item (mabīʿ), and disposing of a sold item before receipt is generally prohibited in Islamic law to prevent risk and uncertainty.
This is a well-established principle in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the specific examples: shirkah and tawliyah
The text specifies partnership (shirkah) and commission agency (tawliyah) as forms of disposal. The author explains that these are the only realistic forms because the musallam fihi is a debt (dayn), so it cannot be sold on a cost-plus (murabaḥah) or discount (waḍīʿah) basis.
Thus, specifying these two examples clarifies how the prohibition applies in practice.
5
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith: 'Whoever makes a salam in something should not change it to something else' (Man aslama fī shayʾ fa-lā yaṣrifahu ilā ghayrihi). The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates impermissibility (taḥrīm).
The chain is aḥad, so thubūt is ẓannī, but the dalālah (meaning) is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
ولم يَجِزِ التَّصَرُّفُ في رأس المالِ والمسلّم فيه كالشَّركةِ والتَّولية قبل قبضه

(۱) قوله : ولم يجز ... الخ ؛ أمَّا عدم جواز التصرُّف في رأس المال، فلأنَّ فيه تفويت القبض المستحق بالعقد، قبل افتراقهما، وهو حق الشرع لا حق العبد ، وأما عدم جواز التصرُّف في المُسَلَّم ؛ فلأنَّ المسلَّمَ فيه مبيع، والتصرُّف في المبيع قبل القبض لا يجوز.
[٢] قوله : كالشركة ؛ يعني كما لا يجوز الشركة والتوليةُ في المسلّم فيه قبل قبضه ؛ لأنه تصرُّف فيه، والتصرُّف في المبيع قبل القبض غير جائز.
فإن قيل : ما وجه تخصيص الشركة والتولية بعد ذكر الأعم وهو التصرف. قلنا : إنَّ أحداً لا يشتري المسلّم مرابحة؛ لكونه ديناً ، ولا وضيعة؛ لكونها ضرراً ظاهراً، ولو اشترى إنّما يشتريه بمثل ما اشتراهُ ربُّ السَّلَم رغبة في كلّه، وهو التولية أو بعضه وهو الشركة، فخصهما ليتصوَّر التصرُّف فيهما من هذا الوجه، كذا في «النهاية».
[٣] قوله : لا تأخذ...الخ; أخرج أبو داود وابن ماجة عـن أبـي بـدرِ شجاع بـن الوليد، حدثنا زياد بن خيثمة، عن سعد الطائي، عن عطية العوفي، عن أبي سعيد الخدري له قال : قال رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «مَن أَسْلَمَ في شيء فلا يصرفه إلى غيره)، ورواه التِّرْمِذِيُّ في «علله الكبير»، وقال : لا أعرفُهُ مرفوعاً إلا من هذا الوجه، وهو (1) في سنن أبي داود (۳) : (٢٧٦) ، و (سنن ابن ماجة»(٢ : ٧٦٦)، وغيرها.
Cream of al-Nihaya for Umda al-Ri'aya
And disposal of the capital and the musallam fihi is not permissible, such as partnership and commission agency, before its receipt.

(1) His statement: 'And not permissible ... etc.'; as for the impermissibility of disposal in the capital, it is because it involves forfeiting the receipt that is due by the contract before their separation, and it is a right of the Shari'ah, not a right of the servant. As for the impermissibility of disposal in the musallam, it is because the musallam fihi is a sold item, and disposal of the sold item before receipt is not permissible.
[2] His statement: 'Like partnership'; meaning just as partnership and commission agency are not permissible in the musallam fihi before its receipt, because it is a disposal in it, and disposal in the sold item before receipt is not permissible.
If it is said: What is the reason for specifying partnership and commission agency after mentioning the general, which is disposal? We say: No one buys the musallam fihi on a cost-plus basis because it is a debt, nor on a discount basis because it is an obvious loss. If one buys, he buys it at the price that the capital owner paid, desiring all of it, which is commission agency, or part of it, which is partnership. So they are specified so that disposal in them can be imagined from this aspect. Thus in al-Nihaya.
[3] His statement: 'Do not take...etc.'; Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah narrated from Abu Badr Shuja' ibn al-Walid, from Ziyad ibn Khaythama, from Sa'd al-Ta'i, from Atiyya al-Awfi, from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever makes a salam in something should not change it to something else.' And al-Tirmidhi narrated it in his 'Ilal al-Kabir and said: I do not know it as marfu' except from this chain, and it is (1) in Sunan Abi Dawud (3): 276, and Sunan Ibn Majah (2): 766, and others.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the prohibition of disposing of the capital (ra's al-mal) and the goods received in salam (musallam fihi) before taking possession. The commentary explains two reasons for this prohibition: disposing of the capital forfeits the right of receipt that is due by the contract before the parties separate, and this right belongs to the Shari'ah, not to the individual. Disposing of the musallam fihi is impermissible because it is considered a sold item (mabi'), and disposing of a sold item before taking possession is generally prohibited in Islamic law. The text then specifies examples of disposal: partnership (shirkah) and commission agency (tawliyah). The author clarifies why these two are mentioned despite the general prohibition: because the musallam fihi is a debt, so it cannot be bought on a cost-plus (murabaha) or discount (wadi'a) basis; rather, the only possible transactions are taking all of it (tawliyah) or part of it (shirkah). Finally, the passage cites a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbids changing the musallam fihi to something else, emphasizing that the goods received in salam must remain as originally specified.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah): A partnership arrangement where the capital owner offers half the capital to another to become partner in the musallam fihi.
تولية (tawliyah): A commission agency where the capital owner transfers the entire musallam fihi to another at the same cost.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or principal amount paid by the buyer to the seller at the time of the salam contract.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or receipt of the item. In salam, it refers to taking delivery of the musallam fihi or retaking the capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is disposing of the capital (ra's al-mal) before receipt prohibited?
A) Because it forfeits the right of receipt, which is a right of the Shari'ah
B) Because the capital might become defective
C) Because it involves riba
D) Because the seller has not yet delivered the goods

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, any disposal of salam capital or goods before physical possession is prohibited (haram) because it violates a shar'i right and is analogous to selling before receipt.

Prohibition of Disposing of Salam Capital and Subject Matter Before Possession
Ruling with proof forbidding disposal of salam capital and goods before taking possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disposal of salam capital and subject matter before possession and identify the hadith that proves it.

Core Idea
In salam, both capital and subject matter are untouchable until physically possessed.

The salam contract involves advance payment for future delivery of goods. Because the capital is paid upfront and the goods are a debt until delivery, both have special status. This lesson examines the strict prohibition against disposing of either before taking physical possession, and how this applies even after cancellation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The passage states that disposal of both the capital (ra's al-mal) and the subject matter (musallam fihi) before possession is not permissible. This includes partnership, assignment, and exchanging them for something else.
The prohibition applies to any transaction that would alienate or encumber the capital or goods before they are in hand.
2
Understand the forms of disposal
The text gives examples: partnership (saying 'give me half the capital to share the goods'), assignment (transferring the right to receive the goods for the same price), and exchanging the capital or goods for something else.
These are all considered disposals that are invalid and sinful.
3
Learn the rule after cancellation (iqalah)
After mutual cancellation, the capital returns to the buyer but retains the same restriction as the subject matter before possession. One cannot buy anything from the seller with that capital until physically taking it back.
This prevents circumventing the prohibition by cancelling and then using the capital immediately.
4
Analyse the hadith proof
The Prophet said: 'Do not take except your salam or your capital.' The imperative indicates prohibition (haram). The exception allows only two options: take the goods if the contract stands, or take back the capital if cancelled. Any other disposal is excluded.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its implication is clear in Hanafi usul.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a buyer who paid cash for 100 bushels of wheat (salam). Before delivery, he cannot sell his right to the wheat to a third party, nor can he, after cancellation, immediately use the returned cash to buy something else from the seller until he physically holds the cash.
The ruling ensures that possession is a precondition for any further disposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يَجْزِ التَّصَرُّفُ في رأس المالِ والمسلّم فيه كالشَّركةِ والتَّولية قبل قبضه، ولا يجوز شراء شيء من المسلم إليه برأس المال بعد الإقالة حتى يقبضه
[۱]
( ولم يَجُزُ التَّصَرُّفُ في رأس المالِ والمسلّم فيه كالشركةِ والتَّولية قبل قبضه ، صورةُ الشَّركةِ : أن يقول رَبُّ السَّلَم لآخر: أعطني نصف رأس المال ؛ ليكون نصفُ المُسَلَّمُ فيه لك. وصورة التّولية أن يقول : أعطني مثل ما أعطيت للمُسَلَّم إليه حتى يكونَ المسلم فيه لك.
ومن صورةِ التَّصرّف في رأس المال : أن يُعطي بدل رأس المالِ شيئاً آخر.
ومن صورةِ التَّصرف في المسلّم فيه : أن يعطي بدله شيئاً آخر. ( ولا يجوز شراء شيء من المسلم إليه برأس المالِ بعد الإقالة حتى يقبضه)، قال النبي : «لا تأخذ إلا سلمك [أو رأس مالك] ) : أي لا تأخذ المسلَّمَ فيه على تقدير المضي على العقد، أو رأس مالك على تقدير إقالة العقد.
وقال عبد الرزاق في مصنفه: أخبرنا معمر عن قتادة عن ابن عمر قال : «إذا أسلفت في شيء فلا تأخذ إلا رأس مالك، والذي أسلفت فيه». وروى ابن أبي شَيْبَةَ في «مصنفه» : حدثنا محمد بن ميسرة عن ابن جريج عن عمرو بن شعيب، عن عبد الله بن شعيب : «إنَّ عبد الله بن عمرو كان يسلف له في الطعام ويقولُ للذي يسلف له : لا تأخذ بعض رأس مالنا، أو بعض طعامنا، ولكن خذ رأس مالنا كله ، أو طعاماً وافياً».
And it is not permissible to dispose of the capital and the subject matter (in salam), like partnership and assignment, before taking possession of it. And it is not permissible to buy anything from the one to whom the salam is made with the capital after cancellation until he takes possession of it. The form of partnership: that the owner of the salam says to another: Give me half the capital so that half the subject matter is yours. The form of assignment: that he says: Give me the like of what I gave to the one to whom the salam is made so that the subject matter becomes yours. Among the forms of disposal of the capital: that he gives something else in exchange for the capital. Among the forms of disposal of the subject matter: that he gives something else in exchange for it. The Prophet said: "Do not take except your salam or your capital" – meaning: do not take the subject matter on the basis of proceeding with the contract, or your capital on the basis of cancellation of the contract. And Abd al-Razzaq said in his Musannaf: Akhbarna Ma'mar from Qatada from Ibn Umar who said: "If you give a loan in something, do not take except your capital and that in which you gave the loan." And Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated in his Musannaf: Hadathana Muhammad ibn Maysarah from Ibn Jurayj from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from Abdullah ibn Shu'ayb: "Indeed, Abdullah ibn Amr used to give a loan in food and would say to the one to whom he gave the loan: Do not take some of our capital or some of our food, but take all our capital or complete food."
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the salam contract. It states that it is not permissible to dispose of either the capital (ra's al-mal) or the subject matter (musallam fihi) before taking possession. 'Disposal' includes actions like partnership (shirkah), where the salam buyer invites another to share the capital for a share in the goods, and assignment (tawliyah), where he transfers the right to receive the goods to another for the same price. It also includes exchanging the capital or goods for something else. The reasoning provided in the commentary is that disposal of the capital would forfeit the required delivery before separation (a right of the Shariah), and disposal of the subject matter is prohibited because it is a sale before possession. After cancellation (iqalah) of the salam, one cannot buy anything from the counterparty with the capital until taking possession, because the capital after cancellation takes the same restriction as the subject matter before cancellation. The proof is the hadith: 'Do not take except your salam or your capital,' meaning you either take the goods if the contract stands, or take back the capital if cancelled. Supporting athar (reports) from Ibn Umar and Ibn Amr reinforce this prohibition. The Hanafi position is that any disposal before possession is invalid, and the capital after cancellation is treated similarly to the subject matter before possession.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid by the buyer in a salam contract; the price advanced for future delivery of goods.
المسلم فيه (al-musallam fihi): The subject matter of the salam contract; the goods to be delivered in the future.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual cancellation of the salam contract by both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
